3.__I Risk, Compliance and Audit Update
Risk
3.1 I MB presented the risk report.
He explained the report was taken from the risks presented in Archer, and that his team would be
meeting GE members/risk associates monthly to review departmental risks going forwards. An updated
risk appetite statement (not updated since 2016) would be presented to the RCC, ARC and Board for
formal sign-off in November, once reviewed against POL’s new purpose (communicated to POL 8/9/20).

2.1

3.2. I He advised that the team had seen increased levels of engagement across the Group due to individual
business units being more aware as the first line of defence, however risk articulation (being clear on the
cause, event and impact of risk), alignment (ensuring risks are managed through Archer as a single
source) and RAG ratings (to ensure they reflect Group standards) are areas for address.

3.3 I The Committee noted that there are 473 risks in the group (listed on Archer) with the following key
enterprise risks:

* Commercial - POL not an attractive business proposition due to complex/confusing products,
new products considered cost ineffective and difficult to scale.
© Covid-19 — the risk to business employees/postmasters and the business remain, particularly in
light of reduced footfall/trading on the high street.
* Financial - concern that funding is insufficient and costs uncontrolled in the short/medium/long
term leading to the inability to deliver strategic objectives.
© Legal — POL unable to comply with legislative and regulatory changes, resulting in fines, lost
revenue, reputational and customer damage. It was noted that legal and regulatory updates
would be provided to RCC to avoid this.
The Committee requested further explanations be provided on the types of risks presented and advised
that some risks had incorrect GE owners for example, AC was listed as risk owner for COVID.

MB advised this would be reviewed with GE members.

Compliance

3.4 I JH presented the compliance report with the following points noted.

Telecoms: JH reported that the regulator had agreed to stop fortnightly reporting following strong call
centre performance during the COVID pandemic.

3.5 I Fairness: A meeting is planned shortly with NR, Meredith Sharples and Ofcom regarding POL’s
performance against the regulator’s Farness Commitments (however JH questioned the need for NR to
attend). The team is working on ways to reduce the number of ‘vulnerable clients’ and plans are to be
presented to the regulator ahead of the meeting.

Additionally, whilst not in breach with the regulator, the Telco team will be sending out Annual Best Tariff
Notifications (required to be sent before 14 February 2021) to customers who have been out of contract
for more than a year and have not received an End of Contract Notification. A rise in customer calls and
complaints is expected as customers query why their current price is higher than the new offers
presented in their communications.

3.6 I GLO/Freedom of Information Requests/GDPR: the team remains stretched responding to Historic
Shortfall Scheme and related/linked FOI requests (186 open requests). Following receipt of 41 FOI I Action:
enquiries considered vexatious from one journalist, 31,000 boxes of data (previously unknown) have now I JH
been identified in storage. These are being reviewed by legal, HSF and Peters and Peters and a separate
paper for ARC will be prepared regarding this issue.

The Committee recognised the need for improved data retention/management training across the group
and in the retail network.

3.7. I Schrems II case and Privacy Shield: the team is monitoring the implications for POL from Schrems Il.
There is the potential that POL will have to update its standard contractual clauses within its contracts
where personal data is stored outside of the EEA. JH remarked this was an industry wide issue and which
the team would continue to monitor.

3.8 I Financial Crime: there continues to be a large increase in suspicious activities reports during lockdown.

4.1_I LCand MC presented the Pensions Assurance paper.
42 I LC reported that since the last meeting, meetings have been held with the unions (who are keen to be

Audit has identified the following lessons learned:

© The 2014 Robin Agreement was not effectively implemented.

* The implementation of success factors in 2018 was to tight deadlines due to licences expiring.

PCI-DSS and Cyber Security Update

PCI-DSS Programme Update

61

6.2

RW presented the PCI-DSS update and reported the following:

P2PE workstream (which encrypts retail and banking transactions from the Pin Entry Device (PED) to a
PCI compliant zone in Ingenico before onward processing to Global Payments (retail transactions) or
VocaLink (banking transactions): this remains on track to deliver per plan the Vocalink accreditation of the
retail & banking software and is followed by a final round of testing and branch piloting activity to be
completed by March 2021 (also on track).

Once pilot activity is completed, the solution will be progressively rolled out across all branches with a full
rollout and formal independent QSA accreditation being completed by June 2021. The team continues to
investigate options to improve the velocity of the roll-out phase timelines.

The TOM workstream (which addresses use of PCI data by POL in processes outside of the transactions
‘occurring at the PED) has identified seven areas where changes to products and processes are required to
achieve PCI compliance.

The activity required to address each of these areas has been planned and the team is working with
suppliers to achieve the required remediation.

The following key updates since the last meeting were noted:

‘* Fujitsu and Ingenico have completed the second banking key milestone: a balance enquiry, and the
banking solution is on track to complete to plan.

* Delivery of the payment solution has slipped by three weeks due to issues with the Global Payments
test environment. Accreditation of the retail payment solution is now scheduled to complete in
October (versus September). As this is not on the critical path there is no impact to the end to end
plan.

* Additional changes have been identified and the project team is working with the business to
understand criticality and potential workarounds. Fujitsu and Ingenico are also impact assessing the
changes in order that these can be delivered in future releases of the software.

* Finances: We are currently tracking within the 2% tolerance on the £15.8m approved at board.
However, some new risks have been identified that may need additional funds.

High Level Design now v2.4 has been updated with further TOM solutions.

Cyber Security

64

Ti presented the Cyber Security update.

Cyber Security Maturity: Cyber security remains a key focus for the team. Since the last ARC meeting,
business and IT strategies have been developing at pace and the team has adopted the following
principles for the 2020/21 cyber programme:

1. Deliver maturity increases where there is greatest value for lowest cost including Cyber Strategy and
Policy developments plus other areas where we are making process-related changes.

2. Align as much as possible of the major cost items with delivery from other IT projects such as EUC,
Belfast Exit where we will gain the capability as part of these projects.

3. Defer the remainder of the higher cost items for maturity increase.

4. Focus on security activities to enable the business such as those around SPM and Belfast Exit.

65

Several capabilities (recommendations in the Deloitte Maturity assessments) have been deferred until
beyond the end of FY2021. These include:

1. DLP (Data Loss Prevention) Phase 3 (Rule Enforcement) - DLP Phase 2 (analysis and pre enforcement
activities) is still proceeding in 2020, and while full DLP maturity takes an average 2 years to achieve,
deferring phase 3 until 2021 will add a further 6 months at a minimum to implementation timeline.

2. Network Access Control (NAC) ~ providing strong defences against unauthorised devices connecting

to our network due to cost.

SOC Forensics — some of this capability will be provided by the GLO IT forensics team.

Firewall attestation capability enhancements enabling clear resolution of firewall rule issues.

Identity and Access Management automation.

SOC Room — unlikely to be needed for 6-12 months

Web Proxy enhancements - similar capability being developed under the EUC project.

Novae

Additionally, the Central risk team is reviewing cyber risk appetite statements considering COVID working
practices, the relaxation of certain controls for extensive remote work practices and the reduction in
capex. Changes in risk appetite will result in changes to target maturity of cyber capabilities.

6.6

Covid-19: ransomware attacks continue during Covid and Deloitte are reviewing the team’s readiness for
such an attack.

The Committee noted that a major cyber security breach test would be completed in late 2020/early
2021, along with tower testing in October/November and a red team exercise.

Insurance Renewal 2020/21

MD and AB (Locktons) presented the Insurance Renewal update.

He reported that the renewal date is 1 November but given timings of the ARC meetings and the
timetable for renewal (insurers will not give terms outside 30 days from renewal), final terms cannot be
provided at this time, Delegated authority would be sought for the CEO and CFO to sign off the insurance
renewal.

As Locktons were procured compliantly (appointed initially for a 3 year term in 2015, extended for three
further 12 month terms), legal and procurement agreed that an OJEU process for insurance renewal was
not required. It was noted that Loctons are incentivised to keep POL’s costs down.

74a

‘AB explained the 2019 renewal had seen difficult market conditions for crime and D&O lines and that
premiums in 2019 had increased significantly to £1.6 million. Additionally POL had purchased further
cyber coverage to mitigate against the increased volume of cyber-attacks.

It was expected that premiums would increase again this year, due to challenging crime and D&O markets
and concerns over COVID claims and whether appropriate coverage is in place. Consideration should be

given as to whether to buy infectious disease coverage under Business Interruption.

The Committee requested that all avenues be explored to reduce the premiums, particularly as working
conditions this year had significantly changed.

The Insurance Renewal Programme paper was NOTED for onward submission to the ARC.

Law & Trends

SIG presented the Law Trends update.

Although noting five matters (listed below) for the Committees’ attention, the Committee discussed the
impact of the Corporate Insolvency and Governance Act 2020 (the Act) to POL.

SIG advised the Act had been fast tracked due to Covid and the potential of insolvency of suppliers. Most
relevant to POL, is the wide-ranging prohibition on the operation of termination clauses in contracts for
the supply of goods and/or services where the counterparty enters a relevant insolvency process. The
new provisions would also prevent POL (as a supplier) from doing ‘any other thing’ (e.g. changing
payment terms) where the client has entered into a relevant restructuring or insolvency process.

However, POL could terminate a contract where a breach occurs after the insolvency procedure had

9. __I Bi-Annual Legal Risk Review (Non GLO/Starling)

9.1 I SIG presented the Bi-Annual Legal Risk Review.

9.2 I She reported on the following key legal risks:

* Contract Management ~ there are differing levels of expertise and management of contracts across
the Group, specifically understanding the obligations of each party and the impact on POL. The Legal
team has developed a contract management framework to improve standards required, and training
will be provided to the business.

Additional training to the legal team will be provided by external legal providers to improve
knowledge/expertise, and an updated Procurement policy has been drafted for ARC for approval.
The development of Web3 (contract management system) would ensure all contracts were centrally
recorded and had named account owners.

AC recommended the contract management process be automised as much as possible and
suggested SIG discuss with TL the financial controls management system (Traction) to use as a
template.

‘* Non-Compliance with Regulatory requirements and Principles - there are varying levels of expertise
across the Group regarding POL’s regulatory obligations where activity is outsourced but not
responsibility. Controls and accountabilities being mapped out by Compliance along with a review of
policies is in place.

9.3 BF noted that litigation was minimal save for GLO/Starling.

The Bi-Annual Legal Risk Review was NOTED for onward submission to the ARC.

11.

Update on Post Master Accounts

11

TP presented the report.

He explained this report arose from an ARC action to understand:

1) What information is presented to Post Masters regarding their customer accounts

2) What trends are prevalent or emerging in Postmaster customer accounts?

3) What further work can KPMG perform to assess whether any other product processes can give rise
to similar issues to those identified with stamps? How much would this cost? What are the
limitations and risks?

Findings from reviewing historical suspense accounts would be presented to ARC in November.

11.2

Regarding information presented to Post Masters in their customer accounts, TP advised these are settled
centrally at the end of a trading period and that branches are expected to complete trading period
accounting 12 times a year on a 5 week, 4 week, 4 week schedule at the end of each trading period.

Letters are sent to Postmasters regarding their balance on the Monday following the scheduled branch
balancing date, with a chaser sent the following week if no response is provided. It was noted that the
letters have been updated since the Common Issues Judgment, and automatic deduction of monies has
also ceased since the judgement, when no response is received from the Post Master.

Revised processes and letters have been independently reviewed and approved by Norton Rose Fulbright
as reflecting the requirements of the Common Issues Judgment.

POL is currently completing a thorough review of its Loss Recovery policies and processes and is
considering other opportunities, such as the use of Branch Hub, to allow Postmasters to monitor the
balances on their customer account(s). The review will conclude in Q3 2020-21.

113

Regarding trends, apart from in TPO4 2020, the year on year balances settled centrally by branches has
fallen year on year in each trading period over the last 20 months. The reduction in balances settled
centrally is driven by a reduction in both the volume of branches settling balances centrally and the
average value being settled centrally.

the total level of declared losses across the network has also decreased, driven by an increase in the
monitoring of branches, an increase in the support provided to branches and through more frequent
earlier intervention for branches that are having operational issues.

114

Regarding the review of further processes that could be identified as having a similar risks to stamps, the
team had identified no further areas.

As no further products/processes had been identified as at risk, TP advised that a high level review of the
product portfolio would be conducted to identify key risk areas. This will include any products with
specific process issues that may have the potential to cause product-related branch discrepancies, and
where Postmasters may suffer detriment as a result.

12.2 I BB presented the Procurement Policy.
She explained that the Procurement policy (not reviewed since 2015) sets out the legal framework for
Public Procurement Regulation under which POL operates as a public sector body. She noted that the
regulations were published in 2015 and have not been altered since, save government notices on good
practice. POL complies with these regulations.
She set out that an Appendix A providing more detailed guidance on Sub Threshold awards has been
drafted for discussion at RCC and ARC, as the regulation and guidance is much lighter and open to
interpretation.
Sub threshold awards is the area which is most often the subject of the recent Procurement Risk
Exception Form (PREN) process. The object of this appendix is to establish the minimum operating
standards relating to the risk management of procurement activity under PCR threshold values. It was
noted the upper threshold is updated every two years to account for exchange rate variations between
the UK and the Eurozone.
A discussion took place on how the regulations are applied in practice and whether how "cross border
interest” is interpreted drives significant additional effort.
A decision on the interpretation of cross-border awards would be made by BF to avoid any internal
confusion, and a review of how UKGI handle cross-border awards (and other similar organisations) would
also be completed.
The Committee recommended a pragmatic approach be taken, particularly with cross-border awards —
whether more legal risk notes should be drafted in future, or a blanket approach to cross-border awards
is taken and the resourcing impact and associated costs assessed.

Risk App:

1. Risk Policy
Policy GE Policy Contact Details Review
Sponsor Owner L f Q History
Alisdair Cameron_I Mark Baldock I/ November 2019

1.1 Purpose and Principles

The Risk Policy is organised around 3 themes: Risk Governance, Strategy and Protocols.
The Policy outlines the minimum control standards that apply to each of these themes. It
is supported by a Risk Management Framework, which provides additional detail and
guidance to support the identification and management of risk across the organisation.

The principles are based on ISO:31000 (Risk Management-Principles and Guidelines)!.

They also have regard to the UK Corporate Governance Code (Guidance on Risk

Management, Internal Control and Related Financial and Business Reporting). The

principles include:

a) Risk management is fundamental to how Post Office is directed, managed and
controlled at all levels

b) Risk must be embedded in all activities. Its underlying risk culture and approach is
key to effective decision making

c) All material risks must be identified, measured, monitored, managed and reported

d) Risk management processes must be aligned and integrated with the delivery of Post
Office’s strategy

e) Risk management must follow a consistent, transparent and auditable methodology

f) Risk reporting must allow for the effective review, challenge and monitoring of risk
exposure against Post Office’s approved risk appetites

g) Risk Governance must adhere to the industry standard ‘3 lines of defence’ model.

1.2 Key definition

The Policy definition of ‘risk’ is based on the ISO 31000 definition namely the effect of
uncertainty on the Post Office achieving its strategic objectives. That effect may be
positive, negative or a deviation from the expected. Risks are described in terms of causes,
potential events and their consequences.

1.3. Application

This Policy applies to Post Office Limited (excluding Post Office Insurance, First Rate
Exchange Services Limited and Payzone Bill Payment Limited although they will have
regard to it) and to all Post Office employees, contractors and business operations.

1.4 Risk Governance

Post Office’s Risk Governance arrangements are concerned with how risks are managed

and reported within the organisation. Risk Governance includes:

. Structure: The Post Office risk management structure consists of the Board, ARC,
RCC and Group Executive (GE)

. Roles and Responsibilities: Post Office follows the industry standard ‘3 lines of
defence’ model with regard to risk management governance, compliance and
oversight

. Risk Reporting: Post Office has in place a reporting cycle for enterprise, intermediate
and local risks. Specific dashboard reports are produced for each GE Business Unit
and the corporate GE every month and the RCC/ARC every 2 months

. Escalation: Where the Risk Owner has insufficient authority to effectively manage
the risk, they should escalate the risk to their relevant GE member, or equivalent
senior leader.

+ ISO 31000 is a family of standards relating to risk management codified by the International Organization for

1.5 Risk Strategy

The Post Office’s Risk Strategy is concerned with specific risk policies and framework,

appetite and attitude to risk, the techniques by which Post Office assess risks as well as

the key priorities in any given year. Risk Strategy includes:

. Strategic objectives: This Policy and the supporting risk management framework is
focused on managing the risks and opportunities of Post Office associated with its
strategic purpose of ‘We're here, in person, for the people who rely on us’

. Risk Appetite & Key Risk Indicators (KRIs): The Post Office’s ‘3 lines of defence’ risk
management structure oversees Post Office’s risk appetite and KRIs. Post Office
assess risk appetite against a 5-tiered scale

. Exceptions: On occasions Post Office may need to accept risks that are outside of
approved appetite or outside of existing procedures. Such circumstances must be
exceptional, time-limited, formally documented (through a Risk Exception Note) and
approved by the relevant GE member.

1.6 Risk Protocols

The Post Office Risk Protocols are the guidelines provided to the organisation for the

management of risk. Risk Protocols include:

. Risk Management Process: The Post Office Risk Management Process is consistent
with the principles of ISO: 31000 and it has a number of sequential components
including Establishing the Context, Risk Assessment, Monitor & Review and
Consultation

. Harm table: The Post Office corporate HARM table describes the risk
impact/likelihood scales (on a standard 1-5 scale) which must be applied

. Risk Hierarchy and Classification: Post Office has a three-level risk hierarchy:
Enterprise, Intermediate and Local risks. The Post Office risks are allocated one of
fourteen categories consistent with those advocated by HM Government's ‘Orange
Book’

. Risk articulation: All risks across the risk hierarchy must be expressed in terms of
their cause(s), the risk event itself, and their impact.

1.7. Minimum Control Standards

Post Office has a number of minimum control standards which apply to the management
of its risks. Below is an example of the relationships between the risk area and the required
minimum control standards:

1. Overview

1.1. Introduction by the Policy Owner
The risk landscape in which Post Office operates is volatile, uncertain and complex,
with increasing demands from its customers and greater scrutiny from regulators,
legislators and Government. This will continue.

This is why Post Office must focus relentlessly on actively monitoring and
strengthening its approach to risk management and promoting a consistent risk-
intelligent culture.

Failure to effectively manage its risks will adversely impact Post Office’s ability to
deliver its business strategy, will undermine the protection and preservation of its
reputation and brand, and the delivery of consistent, high-quality services.

In doing so Post Office acknowledges that risk exists in everything it does. So
everyone in the organisation has a duty of care to manage these risks. However,
risk management is as much about exploiting opportunities as it is about
managing threats. Given this Post Office will inevitably take a certain amount of
risk in order to achieve its strategic objectives.

This Risk Policy (with its clear principles and mandatory minimum control
standards) is an important tool in managing risks efficiently and effectively.

1.2. Purpose

This Policy is organised around 3 themes:

. Risk Governance: This focuses on how Post Office risk management activity
is organised. It describes (i) the relevant Committees (and their respective
roles with regard to risk and how they interact), (ii) the ‘3 lines of defence’
risk management model (and where accountability and responsibility are
placed within. it) and, (iii) the criteria by which risks should be escalated to
provide the requisite oversight;

. Risk Strategy: This focuses on Post Office’s overall approach to risk
management. It describes how risk management activities are aligned, the
level of risk maturity being sought and the level of risk exposure (appetite)
that is acceptable; and,

. Risk Protocols: This defines the risk management activities that must be
undertaken, how they will be undertaken and their frequency.

The Policy also outlines the minimum control standards that apply to each of
these themes.

It is supported by a Risk Management Framework (a series of separate but
interlinked documents) which provides additional detail and practical guidance to
support the consistent and robust identification and management of risk and
opportunities across the organisation.

This Policy, the supporting Framework and other material is accessible on Post
Office’s Central Risk intranet site.

Internal Page 4 of 22 @BCL@2C0975F9

POL00423571
POL00423571

POL-BSFF-0238386_0029
Risk App:

1.3. Principies

The Post Office’s Risk Policy principles are based on 1S0:31000 (Risk

Management-Principles and Guidelines).1 They also have regard to the UK

Corporate Governance Code (Guidance on Risk Management, Internal Control and

Related Financial and Business Reporting)’. There are 7 principles:

1. Risk management is fundamental to how Post Office is directed, managed
and controlled at all levels;

2. Risk must be embedded in all Post Office activities. Its underlying risk culture
and approach is key to effective decision making;

3. All material risks must be identified, measured, monitored, managed and
reported on a continuous basis at an individual and aggregate level;

4. Risk management processes must be aligned and integrated with the delivery
of Post Office’s strategy and in such a way that supports an enterprise wide
approach;

5. Risk management must follow a consistent, transparent and auditable
methodology and proactively recognise external factors, opportunities, and
uncertainties;

6. Risk reporting must allow for the effective review, challenge and monitoring
of risk exposure against Post Office’s approved risk appetites; and,

7. Risk Governance must adhere to the industry standard ‘3 lines of defence’
model to ensure clear accountability and appropriate segregation of duties.

1.4. Application

This Policy applies to Post Office Limited? (excluding Post Office Insurance, First
Rate Exchange Services Limited and Payzone Bill Payment Limited although they
will have regard to it) and to all Post Office employees’, contractors and business
operations.

1.5. Compliance

This Policy is mandatory for all Post Office employees, contractors and business
operations and applies wherever in the world Post Office Limited’s business is
undertaken. All Post Office third parties who do business with the Group (including
consultants, suppliers and business partners) will be required to agree
contractually to this Policy or have their own equivalent Policy.

Where significant non-compliance with the Risk Policy is identified the matter must
be referred to the Chief Finance Officer and the Head of Risk.

is overseen by the Financial Reporting Council.

2. Risk Governance

Post Office’s Risk Governance arrangements are concerned with how risks are
managed and reported within the organisation.

2.1. Structure

The Post Office risk management structure consists of the:

° Board®, informed and advised by the Audit, Risk & Compliance (ARC). They
have overall accountability for the assessment and management of risk,
taking a strategic view of the risks faced by Post Office;

. ARC®. They support the Board in its assessment and management of risks.
The ARC reviews Post Office’s risk policy, risk appetite and attitude to risk to
ensure these are appropriately defined and communicated so that
parameters and expectations are understood; and,

. Group Executive (GE)’. The GE, led by the Chief Executive, has operational
responsibility for Post Office risk management and systems of internal
control.

2.2. Roles and Responsibilities

Post Office follows the industry standard ‘3 lines of defence’ model with regard to

risk management governance, compliance and oversight. This means:

. The GE and Post Office Groups (and their subsidiary business units)
perform the 1% line function. They are accountable for identifying,
assessing, owning and manage their risks. They are also accountable for the
design, implementation and maintenance of the associated internal control
measures;

. The Central Risk team perform the 2"4 line function. They oversee the
corporate approach to risk management. This involves defining and
implementing risk standards, policies, procedures and guidance. They also
assist the 1° line function in the management of their risks in line with good
practice as well as monitor compliance and effectiveness. Furthermore, they
are accountable for reporting to the Board, ARC and GE on Post Office risk
performance as well as advising on emerging risks and changing risk
scenarios; and,

. Internal Audit, who operate independently of 1% and 2" Line functions, are
the 3 Line. They provide an independent evaluation of the adequacy and
effectiveness of Post Office's framework of governance, risk management
and control.

2.3. Risk Reporting
All Post Office risks must be monitored, reviewed and recorded regularly to
determine whether, or not, the corporate risk profile has changed and to gain

5 The Board is collectively responsible for setting Post Office's strategic direction and primary business objectives.
It establishes a robust governance framework and ensures that the Company has financial and human
resources required to achieve its agreed objectives. It is chaired by a non-Executive Director.

©, The ARC is a Committee of the Board from which it derives its authority. It provides oversight of Post Office’s
Group's risk management systems, operational controls and key systems, including monitoring exposures to
the Group Risk Appetite.

7 The GE is a Committee of Post Office senior management responsible for day to day Post Office operational
management.

Internal Page 6 of 22 @BCL@2C0975F9

POL00423571
POL00423571

POL-BSFF-0238386_0031
assurance that corporate risk management rules and procedures are effective.

Such regular (and incremental) reporting has a number of benefits including:

. ensuring responses are effective and efficient;

. building up knowledge to improve risk identification and analysis;

. providing a better link between risks and objectives, key dependencies, core
processes and stakeholder expectations;

. detecting and preparing for changes and trends in existing risks, including
the extent to which risks are aligned with approved appetite;

. identifying and preparing for new and emerging risks; and,

. identifying good risk management practice, building on it and disseminating
it to other parts of the organisation.

Post Office has in place a reporting cycle for enterprise, intermediate and local
risks. Specific dashboard reports are produced for each GE Business Unit and the
corporate GE every month and the RCC/ARC every 2 months. These reports
include (but are not limited to) information on risks trends, compliance levels and
associated actions. The data is taken directly from Post Office’s Governance, Risk
& Compliance tool which provides a ‘single source of truth’.

2.4. Escalation

Post Office has a 3 tier risk hierarchy (see Section 4.3) with each risk having a
designated Risk Owner accountable for proactively managing their risks to within
an approved level of appetite.

Where the Risk Owner has insufficient authority to effectively manage the risk,

they should escalate the risk to their relevant GE member, or equivalent senior

leader. This should be done in circumstances where:

. treatment of the risk requires decisions/actions beyond what the Risk Owner
is authorised to decide and/or there are commercial constraints;

. the risk may impact multiple business areas and/or addressing the risk
requires action by multiple stakeholders;

. addressing the risk requires corporate changes; or,
grievances from stakeholders have been received to which the Risk Owner
cannot impartially and/or effectively respond.

When a risk is escalated, the Risk Owner must provide the receiving manager with
complete information about the risk to enable the latter to act appropriately. Risk
ownership can only be transferred when a risk has been escalated and accepted.
Escalation does not require the impact and/or likelihood rating of a risk to be
‘artificially’ inflated to give it visibility.

2.5. Transfer

Risk ownership can be transferred where the risk has:

. been escalated;

. to be reallocated within the organisation due to internal restructure; or,
. has changed and it needs to be reassigned.

When a risk is transferred, the Risk Owner must provide the new owner with
complete information about the risk to enable them to manage the risk
appropriately. Risk ownership can only be transferred when a risk has been
accepted by the new Risk Owner.

3. Risk Strategy

The Post Office’s Risk Strategy is concerned with specific risk policies and
framework, appetite and attitude to risk, the techniques by which Post Office
assess risks as well as the key priorities in any given year.

3.1. Strategic Objectives

This Policy and the supporting risk management framework is focused on

managing the risks and opportunities of Post Office associated with its strategic

purpose of ‘We're here, in person, for the people who rely on us.’ Such focus

increases the probability of success (and reduces the likelihood of failure) in:

. supporting Post Office Postmasters (i.e. making sure that Postmasters feel
that they have a role in shaping the business);

. building a fit for purpose network (i.e. having the right branches in the right
place); and,

. focusing relentlessly on Post Office core strengths and customers (i.e.
defending and growing main products and services that are so critical to Post
Office income.

3.2, Risk Appetite® & Key Risk Indicators (KRIs)°

Post Office has in a place a series of risk appetite statements and KRIs to:

. allow the Board, GE and ARC to understand the organisation’s aggregated
and interlinked levels of risk to determine acceptability or not;

. provide further assurance that the strategic objectives will be secured and
early warning where these are under threat;

. allow the business to focus limited resource to manage risks outside of
tolerance and tolerate risks which lie within the thresholds;

. support management in making decisions with an understanding of the
degree to which the business is exposed to the consequences of a risk
event; and,

. flex and adapt to the changing business environment.

The Post Office’s approach is based on industry-standard principles*® namely:

. Scope: Risk appetite/KRIs are primarily articulated at the enterprise risk
level and guide/shape the management of linked (but subsidiary)
intermediate and local risks;

. Complex: Risk appetites/KRIs are complex given that excessive simplicity,
while superficially attractive, is counter-productive;

. Measurable: Risk appetites/KRIs are measurable so are based on relevant,
accurate and readily available existing data;

. Flexible: They are not single, fixed concepts. There will be different (and
tailored) appetites for different enterprise risks; and,

® Risk Appetite is the amount of overall risk an organisation is willing to pursue (or retain) to achieve the relevant
strategic objective. It is a qualitative assessment generally made at enterprise risk level.

® Key Risk Indicators (KRIs) are measures put in place to show the underlying trend of specific risks. KRIs are
predictive. They are a set of ‘guide rails’ to provide context within which individual risk impact and likelihood
ratings can be considered. KRIs are a quantitative assessment again generally made at enterprise risk level

1°Institute of Risk Management: Risk Appetite & Tolerance Guidance paper - 9/2011. Gartner: Ignition Guide to
Drafting and Operationalizing Risk Appetite - 2/2020

3.3. Exceptions

On occasions Post Office may need to accept risks that are outside of approved
appetite or outside of existing procedures.

Such circumstances must be exceptional, time-limited, formally documented
(through a Risk Exception Note) and approved by the relevant GE member.

Central Risk report regularly on all open Risk Exception Notes to the GE and ARC.
They are accountable for assuring the relevant business unit are proactively

managing in a timely fashion and the necessary mitigating actions are being taken
to close the exception.

4.

Risk Protocols

The Post Office Risk Protocols are the guidelines provided to the organisation for
the management of risk. These include detailed rules and procedures, how risks

are

classified, as well as the risk management methodologies, tools and

techniques used.

4.1. Risk Management Process
The Post Office Risk Management Process is consistent with the principles of ISO:
31000.

Figure 1: Post Office Risk Management process

It has a number of sequential components:

i.

Establishing the Context: Post Office risks should be understood in the

context of the organisation itself, the activities it undertakes and the range

of skills and capabilities available to it. Consideration must also be given to

the environment in which Post Office operates, its external stakeholders and

their expectations.

Risk Assessment: This is the means by with which Post Office should

evaluate and prioritise the risks it faces through:

a) Risk Identification: Techniques by which Post Office identifies all the risks

its faces be they existing, new or emerging

Risk Analysis: whereby each risk is assessed in terms of its potential

impact and likelihood (see Section 3.2) and its relationship to the

associated approved appetite (see Section 0)

c) Risk Evaluation: a risk profile is produced providing a significance rating
to each risk and therefore a tool for prioritising treatment efforts;

d) Risk Treatment: implementation of actions to respond to risks including
decisions on whether to tolerate, treat, transfer or terminate

Monitor & Review:

This is focused on (a) reacting to early warning indicators of the need to

make interventions, (b) reviewing emerging risks and opportunities, (c)

reviewing whether risks owners are implementing the responses for which

they are accountable and, (d) reporting on the success (or otherwise) of the

4. Consultation: This should ensure that (a) appropriate decisions and actions
are taken to respond appropriately to a particular risk, (b) different areas of
professional expertise and views are appropriately considered in the
management of risks and ( c) sufficient information and evidence to facilitate
risk oversight and decision making are provided.

4,2. Harm Table

Post Office assess each risk by demonstrating the relationship between the
likelihood of the risk materialising (on a standard 1-5 scale) and the impact of the
event should the risk materialise (again on a standard 1-5 scale) to provide an
overall risk rating.

The Post Office corporate HARM table describes the impact/likelihood scales which
must be applied. This is provided at section 9.2.

Each active risk should have 3 ratings namely:

. Inherent: the level of risk before any control activities are applied;

. Current Residual: the latest level of risk considering the effectiveness of
the controls currently in place; and,

. Target: the level of risk that is desired when any planned additional controls
have been implemented. This must. be consistent with the associated
approved risk appetite.

All of these should be recorded on the Post Office’s Governance, Risk & Compliance
tool.

4.3. Risk Hierarchy and Classification

Post Office has a three-level risk hierarchy. These are:

. Enterprise Risks: The Post Office's key business risks are grouped into
fourteen enterprise-level themes which mirror HM Government's approach to
enterprise risk classification (as described below). These risks are Post
Office-wide and so are of corporate importance. Each enterprise risk is owned
by a single GE member. Central Risk provide an update on the management
of these enterprise risks at each RCC and ARC;

. Intermediate Risks: These are sub-categories of an enterprise risk to which
they are linked. They are often the key risks faced by individual business
areas; and,

° Local Risks: These are sub-categories of intermediate risks, to which they
are linked. They are often more specific, local risks faced by individual
business units.

The Post Office risks have been categorised as follows, consistent with those

advocated by HM Government's ‘Orange Book’:

. Strategy: Risks associated with ineffective and poorly designed strategy, not
aligned to the interest of its key stakeholder(s), unable to support the full
integration of any acquired business, delivery of plans or objectives;

‘2 HM Government: Management of Risk (Principles and Concepts) - June 2019

Internal Page 11 of 22 @BCL@2C0975F9

POL00423571
POL00423571

POL-BSFF-0238386_0036
POL00423571
POL00423571

. Governance: Risks associated with ineffective and/or disproportionate
oversight of decision making, unclear authorities and accountabilities across
the management hierarchy, insufficient transparency on business
performance;

. Operational: Risks associated with ineffective and inefficient internal
processes, unsound and insufficiently assured control environment and
ineffective continuity plans and recovery strategy;

. Legal: Risks associated a failure to comply with legislative and regulatory
changes, act in accordance with legal and regulatory obligations (i.e. GDPR),
sectoral regulator guidance and/or other external legal activity (i.e.
litigation);

. Health & Safety: Risks associated with failure to provide a safe work
environment for employees (including workplace and branches) and ensure
physical safety of employees and agents in response to incidents such as
theft, robbery, burglary, terrorism, pandemic and site safety (e.g. workplace
COVID-secure);

. Financial: Risks associated with failure to provide adequate liquidity to meet
financial obligations, having insufficient funding, uncontrolled costs, credit
failure and increased taxation;

. Commercial: Risks associated with failure to implement unattractive
commercial proposition due to high 3. party dependency, capacity
constraints, cost pressures, varied demand and skill shortages;

. People: Risks associated with ineffective leadership and engagement, sub-
optimal culture, inappropriate recruitment model, insufficient capacity and
capability, unattractive Postmasters proposition, industrial action and/or
non-compliance with relevant employment legislation/HR policies;

. Technology: Risks associated with inadequate technology, inability to
deliver the expected IT services and sustain competitive advantage in
products/services;

° Information: Risks associated with failure to produce a robust, suitable,
appropriate, correct and complete data/information and to exploit
data/information to its full potential;

. Security: Risks associated with failure to prevent un-authorised and/or
inappropriate access to the estate and information, including cyber security;

° Change: Risks associated with failure to implement change programmes and
projects aligned with strategic priorities, consistent with budget allocation,
deliver requirements and intended benefits to time, cost and quality;

. Reputational: Risks associated with adverse events including ethical
violations and/or adverse stance to corporate social responsibility; and,

. Marketplace & Brands: Risks associated with failure to maintain a
competitive advantage in response to actions adopted by main competitors,
changes in customers service needs and expectations, loyalty to the Brand.

4.4. Risk Articulation

All risks across the risk hierarchy must be expressed in terms of their cause(s),
the risk event itself, and their impact:

Internal Page 12 of 22 @BCL@2C0975F9

POL-BSFF-0238386_0037
Tab 3.1.1 Risk Policy, including the proposed approach to Risk Appetite & Key Performance Indicators

Impact 1

Cause 2 Impact 2

Impact 3

Impact 4

Figure 2: Post Office bowtie diagram

. Cause: A cause is an element which alone or in combination with other
causes has the potential to give rise to the risk. They are generally (but not
exclusively) external;

. Event: An event is an articulation of the potential adverse or beneficial
circumstances that could result from the cause — in effect the risk itself. A
risk may have multiple causes and consequences and can affect multiple
objectives. Post Office risks should be classified (see section 4.3) against the
Event not the Cause or the Impact; and,

. Consequences/Impact: Consequences are the outcome of a risk event
materialising. Outcomes can be positive or negative. They can also be direct
or indirect. It is also possible to express them qualitatively or quantitatively.
They should be assessed using Post Office HARM table (see Appendix 9.2).

4.5. Governance, Risk & Compliance (GRC) tool
All Post Office risks (across all levels of the risks hierarchy) must be identified,
analysed, evaluated and managed using the Central Risk GRC tool.

4.6. Incidents

All non-IT incidents should be reported by the Incident Reporter. The Incident
Owner responds to incident based on the Post Office risk appetite, identifying the
root cause as well as putting appropriate action plans and all controls in place.

By working closely with the respective Risk Business Partner, the Incident Owner
must also consider any impact the incident has had on the current risks and
controls (mapped where relevant) or whether the incident constitutes a new risk
to the business that has not been recorded within the risk register. The Incident
Owner manages action plans through to closure.

IT related incidents are reported (new) or escalated (existing) through the IT
Service Desk.

5. Minimum Control Standards

Post Office has a number of minimum control standards which apply to the 3.1

7. Governance

Post Office Chief Finance Officer is the Policy sponsor.

Post Office Head of Risk is Policy owner. They are accountable for ensuring that
the Central Risk team provides assurance over compliance with the Policy and the
supporting the Risk Management Framework.

The Policy will be reviewed every year. ARC will approve any changes.

Internal Page 18 of 22 @BCL@2C0975F9

POL-BSFF-0238386_0043
8. Definitions

Appetite
This is the level of risk that the Group is prepared to accept or pursue or before
action is deemed necessary to reduce it; also, sometimes called ‘risk tolerance’.

Control
This is any action taken to reduce the likelihood and/or magnitude of a risk.

Exception

There are on occasion exceptional situations where business requirements make
it necessary to depart from an approved policy and operate outside of risk
appetite. In these circumstances the business can choose to accept this risk and
formally request a risk exception for a defined period of time.

Governance

This is the system by which organisations are directed and controlled. It defines
accountabilities, relationships and responsibilities in the organisation as well as
determine the rules and procedures and monitors performance.

Impact
This is the estimated result including financial, operational and reputational that
would be realised if a risk event would occur.

Incident

An incident is an event, where individual operational controls either did not exist
or did not operate as intended, or the incident was not anticipated, and which has,
or could have, resulted in a financial impact (direct or indirect), a regulatory
implication, a customer consequence or a significant operational impact.

Likelihood
This is the evaluation or judgement regarding the chances of the risk materialising.
Likelihood is also called ‘probability’ or ‘frequency’.

Risk

Risk is defined as the effect of uncertainty on the Post Office achieving its strategic
objectives. That effect may be positive, negative or a deviation from the expected.
Risks are described in terms of causes, potential events and their consequences.

Risk Management

This is the co-ordinated activities designed and operated to manage risk and
exercise internal control within an organisation.

® Tolerance for uncertainty: How willing is Post Office to accept uncertain outcomes? This illustrates the Board's appetite to trade off certainty to achieve a given objective.
A low rating demonstrates the Board's need for certain outcomes, while a high rating shows the Board will pursue an objective even with an uncertain outcome.

* Decision choice: When faced with multiple decision options, how is Post Office willing to select a decision that puts a strategic objective at risk? This question assesses
the Board's acceptance that a given choice may lead to failure to meet a strategic objective. A Board who are averse will only choose options that pose a minimal threat to
the strategic objective’s achievement. A Board open to this risk are willing to trade off the possibility of failure for a high-risk, high-reward decision.

5 Pri tion of strategic objective: How willing is Post Office willing to trade off this specific objective against achievement of other objectives? This demonstrates the
Board's willingness to pursue achievement of a given strategic objective over achievement of another. A Board who are averse to this would never trade off completing the
objective in question for failure of other objectives. A Board which is open would be willing to accept this trade-off.

POST OFFICE LIMITED
RISK AND COMPLIANCE COMMITTEE REPORT

i Risk Appetite & Key Risk Meeting
Title: Indicators: A Proposed Approach Date: 12 November 2020
Author: I Mark Baldock, Head of Risk Sponsor: AL eameron; Group Chief Finance

Input Sought: Noting & Approval

The Risk & Compliance Committee (RCC) is asked to approve that the approach to Risk
Appetite and Key Risk Indicators (KRIs), which will:

* be primarily articulated at the enterprise risk level;

* be assessed against a 5-tiered scale;

¢ be tailored for different enterprise risks;

* take into account our willingness and capacity for taking risks and our level of maturity
in managing them;
be managed using the 3 lines of defence model;
* be reported monthly as part of the Group Executive (GE) Dashboards and every two

months as part of the RCC/ARC Dashboards; and

* be subject to yearly review;

for onward submission to the Audit, Risk & Compliance Committee (ARC).

The Committee is also asked to note that, subject to approval, the approach will be initially
applied to 3 enterprise risk groupings namely (i) Legal & Compliance, (ii) Operational and
(iii) Technology & Security. Their specific application will be subject to separate approval
by the RCC and the ARC, currently targeted for January 2021.

Executive Summary
This paper seeks RCC approval to the Post Office’s approach to Risk Appetite and Key Risk
Indicators (KRIs). It describes:

¢ the principles that underpin the approach

* the associated benefits

* how Risk Appetite and KRIs should be assessed

* how accountability should work

* proposed Next Steps and the indicative timeline

The Post Office’s approach is based on industry best practice.

POL00423571
Tab 3.1.1 Risk Policy, including the proposed approach to Risk Appetite & Key Performance Indicators
Report 34

e What are Risk Appetites and Key Risk Indicators and why are they important?
e¢ What are the principles that underpin our proposed approach?

e¢ How have we approached this work?

¢ What are the proposed Next Steps and the indicative timeline?

Background

1. The Post Office’s draft Risk Policy! is organised around 3 themes namely (i) Risk
Governance (which focuses on how Post Office risk management activity is organised),
(ii) Risk Strategy (which considers the Post Office’s overall approach to risk
management and (iii) Risk Protocols (which defines the risk management activities
that must be undertaken.

2. The Risk Strategy component is specifically concerned with the techniques by which
Post Office should assess risks. This includes an approach to Risk Appetite and
Key Risk Indicators (KRIs) such that we can meet our strategic objectives of (i)
supporting Post Office Postmasters, (ii) building a fit for purpose network and, (iii)
focusing relentlessly on Post Office core strengths and customers.

3. This paper details the Post Office’s specific approach to Risk Appetite and KRIs. These
are defined as follows:

. Risk Appetite: The amount of overall risk an organisation is willing to pursue (or
retain) to achieve its strategic objectives. It is a qualitative assessment generally
made at enterprise risk level.

. KRIs: The measures put in place to show the underlying trend of specific risks.
KRIs are predictive. They are a set of ‘guide rails’ to provide context within which
individual risk impact and likelihood ratings can be considered. KRIs are a
quantitative assessment again generally made at enterprise risk level.

Approach
4. Our approach is based on industry-standard principles? namely:

. Scope: Our Risk appetite/KRIs will be primarily articulated at the enterprise risk
level but will also help guide/shape the management of linked (but subsidiary)
intermediate and local risks;

. Complex: Our Risk appetites/KRIs are likely to be complex. Excessive simplicity,
while superficially attractive, may be counter-productive;

. Measurable: Our Risk appetites/KRIs will be measurable so will be based on
relevant, accurate and readily available existing data;

. Flexible: They will not be single, fixed concepts. There will be different (and
tailored) appetites for different enterprise risks; and,

. Manageable: Our approach will take account our risk management capability, our
willingness and capacity for taking risks and our level of maturity in managing
them.

* The Post Office’s draft Risk Policy v1.1 has been tabled separately for RCC/ARC approval in 11/2020.
2 Institute of Risk Management: Risk Appetite & Tolerance Guidance paper - 9/2011. Gartner: Ignition Guide to Drafting and
Operationalizing Risk Appetite - 2/2020

Benefits
5.

We consider such an approach will:

. allow the Board, GE, RCC and ARC to understand our aggregated levels of risk to
determine acceptability or not;

. provide assurance that our strategic objectives will be secured and provide early
warning where achievement is under threat;

. allow the business to focus limited resource to manage risks outside of tolerance
and tolerate risks which lie within the thresholds;

° support GE in understanding the degree to which the business is exposed to the
consequences of a risk event; and,

. be able to flex and adapt to the changing business environment.

Assessment

6. We propose the Post Office assess appetite (at the enterprise level) against a 5-tiered
scale which ranges from ‘Averse’, through ‘Neutral’ to ‘Open.’

7. Wewill also take into account other factors including our tolerance for uncertainty
(how willing are we to trade off certainty to achieve an objective), decision choice
(how willing are we to make a decision, from a number, that risks a strategic objective)
and strategic priority (how willing are we to trade off an objective against
achievement of other objectives)

8. Our proposed Risk Appetite scale is provided at Appendix 1.

Accountability

9. The Post Office's ‘3 lines of defence’ risk management governance structure will
oversee both the initial application, and ongoing management, of our approved Risk
Appetites and KRIs as follows:

+ Approve all final draft Risk Appetite Statements and associated KRIs.

* Request, as necessary, the Group Executive commission work to articulate

Board/ARC? additional Risk Appetite Statements/KRIS

+ Seek assurance Post Office is adhering to approved Risk Appetite Statements/KRI
thresholds

* Effectively and clearly communicate goals and objectives, strategy, achievement
metrics, and relevant time periods for pursuing the objectives related to developing
risk appetite statements.

Group Executive + Set the Risk Appetites and approved tolerance levels that ensure enough risk is
being taken to ensure Post Office strategic objectives are met

* Secure Group Command consensus on statements and approach.

+ Commission revisions of Risk Appetite Statements and KRIs as needed.

+ Validate or raise concerns about the ongoing viability of existing Risk Appetite

Group and individual Statements approved by the GE /Board

Business Units * Monitor existing KRIs to determine adherence to Risk Appetite Statements that
apply to the Group

* Produce Post Office corporate policy/standards for Risk Appetite and KRIs

* Develop (in discussion with 1* Line) initial set of corporate Risk Appetite Statements
and KRIs

Central Risk * Facilitate ongoing development of new (and enhancements to existing) Risk Appetite
Statements and KRIs

* Provide assurance of 1* Line compliance with standards in this area

* Escalate areas of non-compliance to Group Executive and Board/ARC

3 After initial RCC discussion and approval

. identified as being outside of appetite must be proactively treated with

. assessed as being within appetite can be simply tolerated such that no further
mitigations will be required.

be subject to separate RCC/ARC approval, currently targeted for 1/2021.

Illustrative Commentary

The approved risk appetite for this specific enterprise risk grouping is Averse. This means
the Post Office will avoid taking pretty much any risk in this area where at all possible and
has an extremely low appetite for any such risk materialising.

The current enterprise risk is currently assessed at 2:2 in terms of its potential adverse
impact and the likelihood of it materialising. Although year to date trends show the risk
rating has been reducing in recent months it remains outside of appetite. As such
further mitigations are required in addition to those already in place to reduce the risk
further.

Although 3 of the 4 KRIs are trending Green there is some evidence of volatility in recent
months which, if it continues, will place increasing pressure on the enterprise risk rating
even if, in the near future, it is brought within tolerance.

Comms incident

1.

On 17 September 2020, Fujitsu notified Post Office of a P1 incident affecting inbound
and outbound customer communications between February and September. The total
number of affected inbound comms is 7,846 and the number of affected outbound
comms is 153,684 (following a deduplication process as some comms are issued in
two formats). The outbound comms has impacted c.107,300 unique customers.

Some of the communications are regulated and could result in consumer harm. The Post
Office breach protocol process was followed and Meredith Sharples informed Ofcom
verbally on 27 September of the incident. This was followed up with a detailed letter on
23 October. Ofcom are now reviewing the content of the letter and deciding on whether
to open a formal investigation. A copy of this letter is attached in Appendix 1.

Given the variety of outbound communications that have been impacted, addressing and
rectifying consumer harm has been a complex process as there are 33 different comms
that were impacted which include, but not limited to, cease notifications, overdue payment
reminders, confirmation of engineer appoints. The team have prioritised their work
according to the impact on customers as set out in the table below. Details of the specific
comms impacted was provided to Ofcom.

Priority No. of impacted comms (not customers)

1. Customer's services were ceased 2,877

2. Potential financial impact on the customer 115,830*

3. Customer inconvenienced - communications

relates to regulation 3,650

4. Customer inconvenienced - communications

does not relate to regulation 21,133

5. No customer impact 9,551

* Please note that 99,689 of the priority 2 comms are e-bill notification communications where we inform the customer that their billis
ready online,

The final RCA with Fujitsu is still being worked through. However, we understand that the
main cause of the incident is a result of a file that become too large. Once deleted this
appears to have rectified the issue. Fujitsu have put in further checks to confirm that
outbound comms are going out and inbound comms are arriving correctly.

Of the 7,846 inbound communications, the Fujitsu team has reviewed 2,644 as at 5"
November. Where any consumer harm is identified the customers are being made good,
as if the communications failure had not happened. The Post Office team is checking the
remediation work being done by Fujitsu. We are pressuring Fujitsu to confirm when it
expects to have completed this exercise and are pushing for it to be closed by end
November/early December.

Fairness
6.

Post Office met Ofcom on 20" October to discuss how Post Office is meeting the Fairness
Commitments we have signed up to. Ofcom view these commitments as “reputational
regulation” whereby they publicly expose Communication Providers for practices that they
believe are unfair. Ofcom expressed appreciation for the work done but continued 7

@

reference Post Office having a high pricing differential between in contract and out of

7. Ofcom plan to issue a statement with press coverage on their review of the market early
next year to “name and shame” the worst providers as well as note good practice. We are
expecting further informal follow up questions. Ofcom were understanding that our
current position in the sale process meant that we were limited in our ability to make
changes currently. This means that no action needs to be taken right now. However, once
the sale process/RFP status becomes clearer this will need to be reassessed.

Annual Best Tariff Notifications

8. The telecoms team have delayed the implementation of the introduction of Annual Best
Tariff Notifications from October to December2020, within the regulatory timetable. These
letters will inform customers who are currently out of contract that they could save money
by signing up to a new contract and will present Post Office’s best offers. The ABT must
be sent by 14'* February 2021 and there is a very low risk to the implementation deadline
however the letters are expected to drive more calls to the contact centre and drive
complaints as people will query why their current price is higher than the new offers in
their communications.

9. Ofcom had previously expressed concerns about the amount Post Office customers who
are out of contract. According to Ofcom the average number of out of contract customers
in the industry is 40%, (the highest is 61% and lowest is 24%)!. Post Office have 58%
customers who are out of contract, however some of these customers are on rolling 30
day contracts and would not necessarily be better off moving to a longer-term contract. If
these customers were not included, it is estimated that Post Office would be below 50%. As
noted below, we are due to start sending Annual Best Tariff Reminders to customers,
which we expect will further alter the percentage. Of the customers receiving the ABT
notifications, the Telecoms Team anticipate 5% will churn and 35% of customers will re-
contract onto new pricing. The Team will evaluate the effectiveness of the reminders
before reviewing any further action.

Voice only customers

10. Post Office met with Ofcom on 1% October to discuss their plans for the voice only market.
In 2017, Ofcom reached a voluntary agreement with BT to reduce its pricing for voice only
customers by £5. This voluntary agreement is set to end at the beginning of next year
and Ofcom confirmed during the meeting that they are looking to agree a further voluntary
agreement with BT. We are now awaiting the consultation which will detail the agreement
so that we can assess the impact to Post Office. We expect the impact to be minimal as
Ofcom thought the introduction of retail price regulation would be unlikely and are looking
to have a similar basket control that was agreed before.

European Electronic Communications Code

11. Ofcom have now issued their statement and consultation on the European Electronic
Communications Code and detailed the regulations they plan to introduce. This requires

significant changes to be made in telecoms however Ofcom have staggered the
implementation deadlines, with the first tranche not due to be implemented until

Compliance with Money Laundering Regulations

23. Following the permanent removal of 3,257 and pausing of 2,679 HMRC branch registrations
in May 2020, it has been identified that 6 of the branches that were de-registered as part
of this exercise have completed Travel Money transactions (25 transactions totalling
£6,784.30 between 1* june (HMRC annual registration date) and 28" October 2020).
Post Office is in breach of the regulations in respect of these branches.

24. The IT/Data team have identified historic inconsistencies between Post Office MDM data
and Fujitsu RMD data records, which has caused the issue - a further 35 branch
records are inaccurate, but this has not resulted in transactions to date. A root cause
analysis and correction are still under investigation at the time of writing. We will
investigate where the failure occurred and report back on the appropriate remediation
steps to ensure that branches cannot transact when they are de-registered and/or
what assurance can be provided to reconcile the data between Post Office and Fujitsu.

25. We verbally self-reported the issue to HMRC on 3'¢ November 2020 and have arranged for
the premises to be registered. All of the relevant agent data is held from previous
declarations and can be supplied on the November monthly HMRC report. Given the previous
fine of £800k for premises registration issues in 2016, HMRC may decide to levy a financial
penalty. However, on the basis that there are only 6 impacted branches for a 5 month
period, and limited transactions, we may avoid penalty (previously c.900 premises were
involved going back a number of years). A full written report will be sent to HMRC by 6"
November 2020 once IT/Data investigations have progressed.

26. Suspicious Activity Reports (SARs) and investigations continue to rise, with 1,427 SARs and
157 investigations in August & September (up from 566 and 111 in August & September
2019). This is driven by branches raising concerns with large and multiple cash deposits,
particularly relating to money transmission businesses. There is also evidence of changing
criminal behaviour due to Covid and the economic uncertainty which is likely to further drive
up suspicious activity in our Network and result in more SARs. We are working closely with
Network teams to ensure customer-facing staff are made aware of the risks and their
responsibilities.

27. We continue to work with banks through the National Economic Crime Centre Project
Admiralty and the Banking Framework members to address the risks of cash laundering via
Post Office. We continue to discuss particular activity with 2 banks in relation to significant
cash deposits at Whitechapel and Philpot Street in East London where we continue to see

30. A meeting was held with our new HMRC regulatory supervisor on 30'" September. HMRC
have indicated that they will not be undertaking proactive supervision activity or branch
visits whilst Covid issued continue.

31. A response was submitted to HMT in respect of the Economic Crime Levy Consultation. The
paper indicated that their preferred option is to have a revenue based levy with some
exemptions for small businesses where it is not cost effective to gather the levy. We have
put forward a position that as all POL regulatory revenue is derived via payment of dividend
and commission from FRES, who are also regulated and subject to the levy, this would
effectively be a ‘double-levy’ and therefore POL revenue should be exempt. We are
unlikely to see a response to the consultation, and the draft legislation until the new year.

32. The risk assessment process was presented at the Commercial lead team meeting and have
since seen an improvement in stakeholder engagement.

Anti-Bribery and Corruption (“ABC”) update

33. Annual ABC training has been completed in September - overall completion is at 96.5% as
at 2"? November 2020, however one Business Unit is at 81.7%, and this unit is being chased.

Whistleblowing Update

34. There has been an increase in new reports during August & September 2020 - 8 compared
to 5 in the same period last year. The majority have been received from agent assistants
and relate to allegations concerning Postmaster activity. There are no trends or issues, and
all investigations are proceeding.

35. We are procuring some initial consultancy work with Protect (UK Whistleblowing Charity) to
benchmark our whistleblowing policy and processes and help us to identify if there are
any control gaps. We are also working with the Postmaster Complaints project to ensure
that there is a centralised view of postmaster complaints or reported concerns. We have
provided copies of our policy and procedures to the HMU Public Inquiry team.

External Threats

39. We have identified a growing trend of suspicious high volume Amazon voucher purchases,
relating to potentially vulnerable customers who fall victim to fraudsters persuading them
to purchase high value/large quantities and send to the fraudster. We have issued a number
of communications to raise network awareness and are supporting the Product Owner to
explore options with Amazon and to strengthen current controls in Horizon by limiting the
number of vouchers that can be sold/the amount or provide additional screen prompts for
staff, currently solutions are being worked on, including timescales for delivery.

40. Following investigation into a suspicious activity report, we identified that high risk
suspicious cash deposits from a Money Service Business (a money transmitter) were being
accepted, via our bill payment reseller client Capita. Money Transmission is assessed by the
National Risk Assessment as high risk for money laundering and such companies require
enhanced due diligence. Our investigation of the deposits and activity identified that there
was a complex structure involving numerous businesses between the customer and the
end recipient, with some activity overseas and a similar structure to known money
laundering typologies?. We are actively working with the Product Owner and Capita
to cease the relationship with the businesses involved, and a meeting with Capita is
scheduled for w/c 16th November 2020 to resolve. As a result of this case, we will work
with the Product team to undertake a review of the existing contractual and control
requirements related to Bill Payment re-sellers, and this will also form part of the Payzone

Supply Chain Compliance

41. One of the 3 FTE has been off with suspected Long-Covid, which together with rising
infection rates across the UK has impacted ability to undertake on-site assessments. A
combination of remote checking, interviews, area manager interviews and site visits to
low risk locations has been implemented.

42. The External Approved Contractor Scheme (ACS) assessment in October was completed
remotely with only one observation identified. The score for ACS was assessed as 104,
which is an increase of 10 from last year and maintains Post Office compliance within the
top 5% businesses in the UK requiring this accreditation, indicating that despite the
challenges over the last 7 months, compliance with ACS standards has improved.

43. An independent check of value stock picking accuracy at Swindon was supported. c.13%
of one day’s volume was checked and errors were identified in 1 in 109 items, despite the
items already having been subject to a 100% operational check, i.e. the discrepancy had
not been identified by the unit's own check. The outcome of the independent check has
been escalated to Supply Chain and the Historic Matters Unit for them to remediate.

Compliance Monitoring
47. Mystery shopping had resumed on a small scale from August covering Over 50s cover
and from the start of Q3 covering, savings and travel insurance.

48. Following the UK Government lockdown announcement on 31% October, with the
agreement of our Principals who take compliance responsibility for our FS business,
mystery shopping has been paused. It is expected in any case that sales of FS products
will be reduced for the future period (for example TI sales will be very low/nil).

49. Following the approach of the 1% general lockdown we will use remote measures to review
compliance performance. This includes quality of sales MI that reviews sales peaks,
cancelation and complaints data. Our BAU governance with the regulatory Principals
will continue so that any additional data from them (such as complaints made to the call
centre) can also be reviewed.

50. For the mystery shopping we have undertaken this year the results from protection business
have been generally good and within appetite. For Travel Insurance we will be using the
small number of shops undertaken before lockdown to assess with POI whether any
aspect of the revised sales process needs to be strengthened for future travel insurance
sales when we can hopefully travel again.

are robust and effective in mitigating the risk of error and

Management Comment provided by Tom Lee (Group Financial Controller)

“Iwas pleased to see the report confirmed my expectations, that a robust controls environment exists
over what is an important and complex area of the business. The nature of revenue recognition across
the organisation, driven by high volume of low value transactions with numerous interplays across
systems and stakeholders, will inevitably lead to variances arising. The processes and controlsin place
are designed so as to keep these variances to a minimum whilst seeking to resolve those that do arise
inatimely manner. We will continue to enhance this area and focus our effortsin the short term on
the actions outlined within this report.”

3, Joiners, Movers & Leavers (JML) (Ref.2020/21-01) 3.2
Internal Audit previously reviewed JML processes in 2017,
identifying significant weaknesses in overall governance and

Needs Improvement

Sponsor:

1

0

0
i
A single overarching P1
audit action was agreed
to update the current

to include the gaps

identified during this

Appendix 7

the identification of JML triggers. Failures of JML controls have also
been reported by the external auditors on numerous occasions.

AJIML Programme has been initiated by the business to identify and
address control gaps and weaknesses, with regular progress
updates provided to the ARC. JML controls were also covered
within Cyber Maturity Assessments in 2019 and 2020, which
showed a marked increase in maturity between the two reviews,
with maturity targets being met for two of the four domains
covering JML related controls. However, it is generally recognised
that additional work is required to strengthen and automate access
controls.

The purpose of this audit was to validate the work undertaken by
IT to support the business in identifying and remediating gaps in
the effectiveness of JML processes and to assess the state of JML
Processes across the business, with the intention of ensuring only
authorised users have access to POL systems.

We conclude that IT have been making steady progress in
addressing the JML control weaknesses. Considerable progress has
been made over the last two years to enhance controls, through
the implementation of SmartID, tying the creation and removal of
user access to SuccessFactors, and introducing additional checks
over access to key back-office systems.

However, we observed a continued reliance on manual processes
and insufficient coordination between teams responsible for JML,
privileged access and vendor assurance. The roadmap and action
tracker created by IT will support further improvements in JML
processes and we have highlighted additional actions that should
be included in the plan.

We consider the residual risk of unauthorised access to POL
systems to be substantially mitigated by the controls currently in
place, but based on the actions still required, we have rated this
report ‘Needs Improvement’.

Management Comment provided by Tony Jowett (CISO)

“This reportis very timely and helpful in our quest to sort JMLin Post Office. It re-emphasises that
effective JML requires HR and IT to work closely together and the review of all in scope is very
welcome. We agree with the action that we must create a roadmap/plan and would add an additional
one about creating a picture of JML that could serve as a dashboard. Many thanks to IA for shining a

lightin this area.”

4. Branch Hub (Programme Assurance) (Ref.2020/21-12) 3.2

The ‘Branch Hub’ programme was initiated in 2018 to delivera
new digital self-service channel, to replace or support traditional
channels for the interactions between postmasters and back-office
functions. The overall delivery of the minimum viable product
(MVP) and the adoption by Postmasters proved challenging, as
noted in previous audits.

Needs Improvement In February 2020, the IC approved Branch Hub's move from the
bespoke Fujitsu product to the ServiceNow platform. Due to
Sponsor: COVID-19, the programme focused on delivering functionality to
Dan Zinner support Post Office’s response to the pandemic, including ‘Change
Branch Hours’, ‘Agent Remuneration’ and ‘PPE Ordering’. This has
Audit actions: driven adoption to surpass 9,400 users. The delivery of further

features has been divided into three increments. As of August
2020, a total of £20.6m had been invested in the Programme.

ie}
6 The purpose of this audit was to re-assess the operating
0 effectiveness of programme delivery while ensuring previous audit
6 recommendations have been adopted.
Branch Hub has now demonstrated that it can deliver and that its
Appendix 8 underlying platform and delivery model are sound. Since its reset

in February 2020, Branch Hub has been primarily driven in a short-
term tactical way to prove it could deliver. This strategy was
broadly successful as Branch Hub delivered its main objectives.

However, it did not fully succeed in overcoming the inherent lack of
trust and further constrained resources which resulted in some
previous audit weaknesses not being fully addressed. We once
again note that the programme lacks strategic direction and a
joined up approach on how future features should be linked. GLO
considerations have not been fully considered and embedded. We
also noted additional controls weaknesses associated with the new
delivery model. These gaps impacted on the overall programme
performance, contributing to the present challenges in fully
unlocking the committed FY20/21 benefits.

Title: PCI DSS Compliance Meeting Date: I 12th November 2020

Joseph Moussalli, PCI DSS Jeff Smyth, Interim Group
