The Committee is asked to note:
« What progress has been made during the last reporting period?
e What are the key risks?
for onward submission to the Audit, Risk & Compliance Committee (ARC).

Previous Governance Oversight

The Risk & Compliance Committees (RCC) has requested a rolling update on PCI-DSS
programme progress.

Executive Summary
The programme consists of 2 core delivery streams:

1. The Point-to-Point Encryption (P2PE) workstream, which encrypts retail and banking
transactions from the Pin Entry Device (PED) to a PCI compliant zone in Ingenico before
onward processing to Global Payments (retail transactions) or VocaLink (banking
transactions).

2. The Target Operating Model (TOM) workstream which addresses use of PCI data by POL
in processes outside of the transactions occurring at the PED.

Over the course of the last period, 2 key milestones have been completed culminating in the
demonstration of a banking transaction going end to end between the PED and VocaLink.
Additionally, we have completed accreditation of our payments solution and are scheduled to
start a payments pilot through Global Payments on five counters in November

Overall the programme remains on track to begin a pilot with both payments and banking
transactions in March. However, due supplier capacity constraints (in part due to COVID), it
was agreed at the PCI programme steering committee that the Travel Money Card solution will
be delivered as a subsequent release. Unless these constraints can be addressed, and more
capacity becomes available, the timeline for a complete audit will move back to August ‘21.

The Target Operating Model workstream is progressing well and has progressed the secure
solution needed for the Financial Crime team and a solution for processing BIN ranges greater
than 6 digits.

Target Operating Model (TOM) Workstream Update

The focus of the Target Operating Model workstream is to review the Products and
Processes that use Card Holder Data and to remediate and remove them from the scope
of the PCI DSS audit where possible. If removal is not possible then PCI DSS controls will
be put in place. A plan has been prepared to remediate the products and processes.

« An additional TOM release has been planned this period - Release I: BIN >6 digits
workaround. Post Office products use BIN ranges greater than the six digits but the
Ingenico product does not process more than six digits for PCI compliant systems. The
Post Office team has worked with the business to adjust products and processes.
However, a substantial workaround solution still needs to be put in place. This
workaround is now being commissioned with Fujitsu, ATOS and Vocalink.

e High Level Solution Design HLD v2.6 has been approved by EAG. It includes the
solution for Travel Money Card using Ingenico tokenisation. This solution has been
agreed with FRES.

e Release A: Obfuscation of PCI data on screens and receipts. The solution has been
developed and deployed into production.

e Release B: Telephone payments. The build has been deployed into production ahead
of schedule.

« Release F (Quatrix): 14-day retention policy implemented on Quatrix. First Quatrix
scan completed (14/08). Results indicate that there are no unauthorised processes
using PCI DSS cardholder data. Mimecast (risk raised last month): A way forward has
been agreed on how to implement alerting rules and a request has been made to
Computacenter to implement DLP rules.

« Release G: PCI DSS compliant solution agreed with EAG for providing full PAN for the
Financial Crime and Banking enquiries team. Accenture has been selected to
implement the Ingenico detokenisation application that will be hosted in AWS.

e Release I (New): BIN>6 processing workaround. Work orders being agreed with
suppliers.

« Success Factors: Support and Support Plus training modules were released onto
Success Factors for Information Security and Data Protection to incorporate PCI DSS
training requirements.

2. What are the key risks & issues on the programme?
The following are identified as key risks:

Risk: There is a risk that any changes needed to the Fujitsu/Ingenico software will impact
the plan. Fujitsu and Ingenico have given a commitment to meeting the current timescales
on the basis of no further changes.

Mitigation: Post Office has managed to descope some of the changes identified by
working with the business. At steerco it was agreed to implement two of the essential
changes — BIN>6 processing workaround and Ingenico tokenisation. TMC could not be
accommodated and so this will be delivered as a separate release. The steerco decision
aims to keep the implementation of the P2PE solution for banking on track but there will
be a delay to the PCI DSS compliance completion.

Risk: There is a risk that Santander cannot migrate services to route through Vocalink
within the timescales.

Mitigation: Post Office team is working closely with Santander to produce proposals
including costs and timescales. Santander has allocated resources to scope the work but
this is running at a slow pace. The Post Office Director of Banking Services has been made
aware of the issue.

Risk: There is a risk that Coronavirus may impact the delivery timescales for any supplier
across the entire PCI programme.

Mitigation: Post Office is working with all engaged suppliers to better understand their
contingency plans to ensure that delivery momentum is maintained. This includes the
examination of options to minimise delivery impact by understanding key delivery person
risks, supply chain risks and other indirect or latent dependency factors.

Issue: Due to the complexity of the solution further funds will be needed. Fujitsu is
forecasting an overspend of 10% - £504k; further funds will also be needed to
accommodate the change in delivery approach.

Mitigation: Post Office has challenged the forecast overspend with Fujitsu and will
continue to track and manage this issue. Further funding will be needed.

5. The roadmap for the cyber activity is show in Appendix 2.

Securing Cloud for Belfast Exit
6. As part of the Belfast migration a key requirement is, how do we secure the cloud and
demonstrate we are at least as secure today if not more so in AWS than Belfast.

7. Firstly, we need to understand AWS public cloud offering.

AWS operates a shared

responsibility model between its customers and AWS; the below diagram illustrates where
the responsibility sits for each organisation
a. Post Office is responsible for all the services we consume and build on top of the
shared software and hardware provided by AWS. “We’re responsible for
security in the cloud”

b. AWS is responsible for protecting the infrastructure that runs all of the services
offered in the AWS Cloud. This infrastructure is composed of the hardware,
software, networking, and facilities that run AWS Cloud services. “AWS is
responsible for security of the cloud”

Confidential

Post Office Limited - Risk and Compliance Committee-12/11/20

79 of 226

POL-BSFF-0238386_0078
POL00423571
POL00423571

Tab 4.2 Cyber Security

CUSTOMER

8. AWS responsibility layer removes the management and security responsibility of a physical
data centre, therefore allowing the Post Office to focus time and recourses on protecting
our digital environment without the burden of a physical location. AWS is certified to;

a. ISO 27001 internationally-recognised standard for security management best
practices and comprehensive security control

b. Service Organisation Controls (SOC) 2 Report - evaluates AWS controls meet
the criteria for security, availability, and confidentiality

How has the Post Office worked to delivery security?

9. Securing the cloud is a key responsibility of the CISO, the cyber security team has been
integrated throughout the entire migration, managing the security delivery process;
a. Ensuring the delivery of infrastructure & applications follows Post Office
repeatable secure by design processes.
b. Collaborate with AWS professional service consultants and trusted 3rd parties
c. Implemented AWS Cloud Adoption Framework (CAF) which is AWS methodology
for deploying securely into the cloud, focusing on the following security areas
i. Identity & Access Management
ii. Logging & Monitoring
Infrastructure Security
iv. Data Protection
v. Incident response
d. Aligned Post Office Security Control Framework, based on NCSC (National Cyber
Security Centre) 14 Cloud principles for alignment to the wider business.
e. Conducted independent 3rd party IT Health Checks & Penetration tests on
services delivered into the cloud.

Cloud Security Maturity Assessment

10. We instructed a 3rd party to perform an independent cloud security maturity assessment
Six months into our cloud journey to:
a. Identify areas of strength in our approach to cloud security
b. Identify current gaps in maturity and the resultant recommendations
c. Define tactical and strategic objectives to improve maturity

11. We achieved an average score of 3.0 on the Capability Maturity Model Integration scale

(CMMI) with a rating of Defined. Illustrating we have built a solid foundation with
standardised repeatably processes, which will allow us to continue to mature.

Approach to ongoing security assurance

deliver further services.

Report: What are the results of the recent fake phishing attack and
recommendations?

15. Phishing attacks are most used as successful forms of cyber entry point into an
organisation. Using social engineering and phishing methods, cyber criminals rely on
people to gain access to an organisation. This is orchestrated by delivering malware
through emails as attachments or links which download malicious content on to a users’
machine or the network when clicked. To mitigate, Post Office replicates real-life phishing
simulation campaigns to understand the risk exposure and to educate colleagues.

16. Our stats for a fake phishing campaign in October 2020 are shown below:
Emails sent 4,047
Total colleagues reporting as a scam 1,154 4.2
Total colleagues “lured” *391
Post Office security measures initiated (average) 73 seconds
Total colleagues lured before mitigation 6
Skipped Training 178

17. For our defences to work correctly then we need users to report phishing emails rapidly
so that others do not then receive the emails - the AI engines can only do so much and
need user help to be accurate.

18. The two critical measures therefore are time taken for our security measures to recognise
this specific attack as dangerous (73 seconds) and the number of colleagues who clicked
or opened the attachment BEFORE these measures kicked is 6. This means that we could
be dealing with 6 infected machines. Where payload from the attack included ransomware,
then any one of those infections could result in a serious hit on our ability to operate as
an organisation. The ransomware could have the ability for lateral movement and to
replicate itself across Post Office.

20. Compared with recent fake attacks we have seen slight improvement towards reporting
of phishing emails, but on average across all he fake campaigns about 45% will read
phishing emails but not report it. Approximately 34% will report a phishing email.
Approximately 17% of lured users who read and clicked the email, only 6% will commit
to completing recommended training. This signifies a need to close the gap between
people reading the email and those who report emails. Crucially for those who click on
phishing emails, the gap between lured and training should be close to 0.

Tab 4.3 Joiners, movers, leavers
POST OFFICE LIMITED
RISK & COMPLIANCE COMMITTEE REPORT
Title: Joiners, Movers, Leavers Update Meeting Date: I 12 November 2020 43
. Tony Jowett, Chief Information . . .
Author: Security Officer Sponsor: Jeff Smyth, Interim Group CIO

Input Sought: Noting

The Committee is asked to note the status and plans regarding the reduction of risk associated
with Joiners, Movers and Leavers (JML) - in particular those associated with Movers during the
current hybrid operating model, for onwards submission to the Audit, Risk & Compliance
Committee (ARC).

Previous Governance Oversight

Actions to report on this occurred at the previous Risk and Compliance Committee (RCC)
meetings.

Executive Summary

In our continued focus on Joiners, Movers, and Leavers we have performed a deep dive on the
Movers process, following on from the previous report’s focus on Leavers. Our analysis covers
a period for which COVID-19 continues to be actively changing our operating model and
introducing new challenges. Short term and longer-term actions are included for movers and
an update is provided on the actions raised in the previous paper.

In addition, we have worked closely with Internal Audit who have reviewed JML across the
business and IT platforms. In line with this review we are drawing up a roadmap and plan for
improving JML within Post Office to give further clarity on dates, achievement, and
operationalisation of good JML practices.

Confidential

88 of 226 Post Office Limited - Risk and Compliance Committee-12/11/20

POL-BSFF-0238386_0087
POL00423571
POL00423571

Tab 4.3 Joiners, m

Questions addressed

1. What are the complexities that we need to address associated with the Movers
process?

How well have managed those complexities during the period July-September 2020?
What progress have we made on the actions identified in the last paper?

What are the key points and actions arising from the IA audit of JML?

What are our next step

Report

4.3

OF BrGe! Ny

1. In this report we are focusing in on the Movers process for staff and contractors as this
presents the next greatest risk to the business to Leavers and is a process that is likely
to be tested during the current and future Organisation Development activities.

What are the complexities that we need to address associated with the Movers
process?

2. Effective moving of role in POL is reliant on that the old- and new-line managers
informing People Team and IT what is happening and what corresponding data access is
required. Guidance is available for line managers with a detailed checklist on the Post
Office Intranet and People Team business partners reinforce this process.

cm For any Mover the ideal behaviour is that the access rights associated with the old role
are dropped and then rights are created in line with the needs of the new role.

4. The Movers process needs to cope with the different types of mover covering at least:

a. Formal role move resulting from a vacancy being made available, interviews and
job award

b. Formal secondment for up to a year from a substantive role

c. Informal secondment to cover a temporary absence or gap

5. There is a heavy reliance on line management in Post office to run the movers process.
Informal and Formal Secondments do not get tracked routinely on Success Factors - -
they only appear if line management remember to do it. Likewise, any associated
changes to IT data access — they only get changed if line management remember to do
it.

6. Good movers practice would also be backed up by a periodic data recertification activity
that would pick up an movers data access that was incorrect.

How well have managed those complexities during the period July-September 2020?

7. People Team do not routinely produce a report regarding movers in the same way they
do for leavers. The lack of such a report also means there is no routine oversight or
auditing of this process. Success is therefore heavily dependent on line managers doing
the right thing at the right time.

Confidential

Post Office Limited - Risk and Compliance Committee-12/11/20 89 of 226

POL-BSFF-0238386_0088
POL00423571
POL00423571

Tab 4.3 Joiners, movers, leavers

@

8. The People Shared Service Centre (PSSC) were able to produce a custom report showing
those whose role title had changed and from this a subset of people who were movers
was identified manually. By this analysis there were 34 people who had moved role
during this period - on average one every other day.

9. For each of those 34 people a check was performed with IT service desk to see if any 4.3
data change requests had been made by line management - as per guidance on POL .
intranet. The IT service desk confirmed that no such requests had been received for
these individuals from anyone.

10. In the end user computing environment data recertification checks are not performed or
coordinated centrally - they are reliant on individual data owners doing their own checks
as and when they deem it necessary.

11. The recommended improvement actions are:

a. PSSC to produce a monthly mover’s report (like the leavers report) to go to each
GE area for confirmation by line management and to go to IT Service Desk
(Target Jan 2021)

b. IT Service to develop the ability to automatically drop a mover’s access and
create new access in line with the new role (Target Jan 2021)

c. Develop a fit for purpose data recertification mechanism to catch any
inappropriate data access (Jan 2021)

d. People Team to continue with efforts to move PSSC to Service Now enabling
case management across People Team and IT (previously identified action -
dates as per roadmap/action plan and in progress with approvals)

What progress have we made on the actions identified in the last paper?

12. In the last paper we identified a series of actions around JML. Of these 11 were due by
end October.

13. Of the 11 due for completion
a. 6 have been completed
b. 4 are in progress with estimated completion dates in November. These are
i. Recruitment of JML/Controls analysts x 2 - we are searching for suitable
candidates
ii. Production of GE leavers report - with PSSC to produce draft report
iii. Privileged access additional approvals by CIO/CISO (feasibility being
investigated with PAM team),
iv. Spot auditing of 3rd parties where we currently have attestation only
(resource offered role and starting in December)
c. 1 pending PRB approval (PSSC move to SNOW)

Tab 4.4 Belfast Datacenter (Horizon) Disaster Recovery Post Test Briefing

POST OFFICE LIMITED
RISK & COMPLIANCE COMMITTEE REPORT

fn. Briefing on August Belfast data m .
Title: centre Disaster Recovery Meeting Date: I 12 November 2020
Author: Gary Walker, IT Director Sponsor: Jeff Smyth, Group CIO

Input Sought: For Noting

The Committee is asked to note the follow on plan regarding the Belfast datacentre disaster
recovery test.

Executive Summary

1. The Fujitsu Belfast datacentre currently hosts Horizon and other business critical
applications used to deliver branch counter services. Disaster recovery failover testing of
the estate is due annually. The previous test was performed in October 2019 and for 2020
the test date was agreed as August 29th 2020.

2. In 2019 the original test was to fail over on Saturday evening, deliver services from the
disaster recovery secondary site on Sunday, then fail back Sunday evening. This test failed
and a plan was put in place to remediate and re-test. This re-test was completed in
October 2019 but with a reduced test scope carried out over only one night which was
successful.

3. For 2020, it was agreed that in addition to the original failover and failback plan from
2019, we would run services for a full day of trading in the secondary (disaster recovery)
site before failback to the primary Data Centre.

4. During the 2020 test, failover to the secondary datacentre was completed within 4 hours
but this was 2 hours behind schedule due to a delay in failover of core networking
components. Fujitsu (post-test) subsequently completed investigation into the root cause
and mitigating actions have now been implemented.

5. Testing during failover resulted in 48 of the 51 tests completing successfully. There were
three Services which did not pass testing: (i) Debit Card transactions via the TNS service,
(ii) Telecoms and (iii) National Express transactions. Due to the 2 hour delay in failover,
there was limited time to troubleshoot the failed tests. As Debit Card transactions were
deemed a critical service for trading a decision was made for trading on Sunday on the
secondary environment to be abandoned and failback to production. This was to ensure
that all services would be available for start of day on Sunday.

6. The risk position is considered to be unchanged following this test as subsequent to the
test Fujitsu have investigated and implemented mitigated for all failed components.

Confidential

Post Office Limited - Risk and Compliance Committee-12/11/20 93 of 226

POL-BSFF-0238386_0092
POL00423571
POL00423571

Tab 4.4 Belfast Datacenter (Horizon) Disaster Recovery Post Test Briefing

@

7. Four options are available as part of our next step actions and one of these has been
recommended which is to complete only failed component testing and defer wider testing
to an appropriate point in the Belfast Exit programme

Questions addressed

¢ What is the result of the 2020 failover test?
e Has the test altered the risk position?
e What are the next steps?

Report - What is the result of the 2020 failover test?

8. Due to Covid the test was deferred from the early part of the year which required a
reasonable amount of re-planning. The planning for the test commenced 10 weeks before
the test date, working with key business stakeholders and partners. Model Office were
fully engaged to ensure full regression testing across the platform. No major obstacles
were encountered in the planning however it was challenging to get full Business User
engagement for testing across all the services in scope.

9. A POL change freeze was implemented to take effect just prior to the event, but some
confusion ensued as this was out of alignment with Fujitsu’s change freeze. Better
alignment on this matter is needed in future and it has been noted as a lesson learnt.

10. Pre-failover baselining was completed successfully on Thursday and Friday (27th and
28th). All Go/No Go stage checkpoints were attended by IT Senior Stakeholders, Business
Representatives, POL Test Teams and partners. No blockers to a Go decision was voiced
on any occasion and Failover commenced as planned 19:00, Saturday 29th. A Gantt chart
showing progress versus plan is shown in the Appendix 1.

11. The comms plan was executed well with good communications and effort throughout from
all parties and partners. However, three Clients (Santander, Camelot and GlobalPay)
reported issues during the planned outage. It was subsequently confirmed that they had
been properly informed of the test timelines and impact, but they had not passed on this
notification internally. Positive confirmation from all partners has been added as a lesson
learnt for future tests.

12. Call bridges worked well with timely attendance noted and helpful input provided by all as
needed. Hourly reports were produced via Horice as an aid to monitoring stability and
availability during the execution of the test which provided an active view of the entire
transactional state across all branches and counters.

13. The plan expected the Core Services (banking) to be available at approximately 23:30.
Despite being approximately 30 mins ahead of schedule early in the plan Fujitsu informed

us at 22:38 that we were no longer ahead of schedule due to key network components
taking longer to failover (network stabilisation issue).

Confidential

94 of 226 Post Office Limited - Risk and Compliance Committee-12/11/20

POL-BSFF-0238386_0093
POL00423571
POL00423571

Tab 4.4 Belfast Datacenter (Horizon) Disaster Recovery Post Test Briefing

@

14. At 23:30 Fujitsu informed us that that they were now running 1 hour 10 mins behind due
to further restarting of underlying infrastructure to rectify the network stabilisation issue.
A follow up review was scheduled for midnight.

15. At 00:00, Fujitsu indicated the ETA for Core Services was now 01:30. The progress was
monitored closely, and core services were made available at 01:00.

16. After some initial slowness, testing commenced with notable results which included
Banking services being tested successfully at 01:30 am.

17. Testing of all other services commenced testing at approximately 01:45 where it was
reported that (i) debit card (TNS) transactions were not working. Two further test failures
occurred for (ii) Telecoms and (iii) National Express were reported.

18. The delay in the failover of data centres (due to the network stabilisation issue) resulted
in very limited time to diagnose and correct any issues by the ‘point of no return’ deadline
of 2:30 am, at which point the decision had to be made to fail back or remain in the
secondary disaster recovery site for Sunday trading. As no fix for the Debit card issue was
imminent, at 2:15 the decision was made to fail back to the primary datacentre to ensure
full service availability for Sunday trading.

19. The failover back to the Primary Data Centre was very successful being completed ahead
of plan by 1.5 hours, with no issues (see Appendix 1). The majority of batches completed
successfully with no processing issues found. Two files sent via the PODG (Post Office Data
Gateway) system were delayed by approximately one day due to the nature of how they
are created (they require input from other 3rd parties). It was agreed that deeper analysis
of impact to PODG should be done in future to allow better communication to processing
parties in future tests. This has been noted as a lesson learnt.

20. Horice was utilised on Sunday to double check that all transaction types were completing
successfully with volumes within normal Sunday trading levels.

21. Fujitsu agreed to perform full root cause analysis, evaluate and to advise in due course
with the expectation that remediation will be made as needed. At the time of writing this
report, the root cause of all failed elements have been rectified.

22. Post Test Weekend Observations and Comments have been documented in Appendix 2.

Report — Has the test altered the risk position?

23. Overall it is considered that the Fujitsu risk profile has not changed materially as a result
of this test. 48 out of 51 Services did (functionally) failover and all services failed back and
were successfully tested. During the failover, test transactions done in Model Office were
successfully replicated to the secondary site and evidence could be seen of these being
stored successfully on the Horizon database. Though the test cannot be considered 100%
successful, the failed components would not prevent POL from being able to meet key
service delivery obligations in response to a disaster.

Report - Next Steps

25. There are four options available to us with regards to further risk mitigation (if required).
Our recommendation and current plan is to proceed with option d:

a) Assurance of Failed components
Do not conduct a full failover again but seek to verify the 3 components that failed in
isolation. This will provide confidence that the components that failed have been
corrected. This gives nil cost impact as these activities can be tested in BAU. There
will be only minor interruption to BAU during testing. Note that 2 of the 3 components
were successfully tested in October after having the root cause of failure fixed.

b

5

Failover to secondary disaster recovery site, re-test failed components only

Conduct the failover again, but only retest the remediated components and then fail
back. We would not plan to run for the day in the secondary disaster recovery site.
This provides only marginally more risk assurance than option (a). Estimated cost of
£40,000 with expected outage to business of 8 hours over a Saturday night. Given
proximity to peak period, this would need to be done in 2021 but with close liaison
with the Belfast Exit programme as the underlying estate is fundamentally changing
as this programme continues to execute.

Cc

LS

for full day Sunday trading

Repeat the test with the full scope, including running on the secondary disaster

would be disruptive to the business as well as carrying the highest cost. Estimated
cost of £80,000 including additional supplier support and Project Management
resource to run the event. Expected outage to the business would be 4 hours Sat
night, 5 hours Sunday night and potentially more. Given proximity to peak period,
this would need to be done in 2021 but with close liaison with the Belfast Exit
programme as the underlying estate is fundamentally changing as this programme
continues to execute. The next appropriate long weekend would be over Easter.

d) Complete only failed component testing and defer wider testing to Belfast
Exit

(option a), then move the scope of the wider disaster recovery test into the Belfast
Exit programme of work to occur once the virtual datacentres are ready. Once ready,
run an equivalent disaster recovery exercise. There would be no cost impact as this
activity is already covered under the Belfast Exit programme. However, the exercise
will look materially different to the existing physical test as the majority of services
will be resilient by design and therefore will only need limited testing

Input Sought:

The Committee is asked to note the findings from KPMG's review of Post Office’s historical
operation of suspense accounts for onward submission to the Audit, Risk & Compliance
Committee (ARC).

Previous Governance Oversight

.

Post GLO Settlement Programme SteerCo of 1 July 2020.
Risk & Compliance Committee (RCC) and ARC of 13 July and 27 July 2020, respectively.

Executive Summary

1.

Allegations continue to be made that Post Office may have recovered sums from Postmasters
which were not ‘real losses’ to Post Office as they were housed in suspense accounts and
taken to profit by Post Office.

. KPMG were instructed to review Post Office’s current operation of suspense accounts. Given

the robust and transparent investigations process that is undertaken, their principal finding
was that how these suspense accounts are operated today should not result in Post Office
pursuing Postmasters for sums it had or could eventually take to profit (nor had any evidence
been presented to indicate this had happened).

. The ARC approved instructing KPMG to also review Post Office’s historical operation of

suspense accounts. This review involved looking at two suspense accounts - the Agent
Creditor Suspense Account and the Customer Creditor Suspense Account.

. The Agent Creditor Suspense Account. From their review of historical practice, KPMG has

found no evidence to suggest that amounts posted to this account relate to discrepancies
which should have been repaid to Postmasters.

. The Customer Creditor Suspense Account should only include sums that relate to outstanding

/ unmatched customer funds. KPMG's finding is that overall, a robust resolution process
appears to have been in place for each product type that is posted into this account and
branch affecting discrepancies should not be included within this account, which is a holding
account for customer’s money. However, KPMG has identified two exceptions — both of which
are caused by Postmaster error or failing to follow prescribed process:

i, Where Postmasters have accepted cheques made out to Post Office as payment for
certain services but have incorrectly recorded this transaction as having been paid for

6. KPMG has advised that further investigation into these two potential issues is difficult given

especially in relation to quantification to that already included in their report and this paper.
Further, if a branch were to have made such errors, they would manifest themselves as
shortfalls which if they dispute, the Historical Shortfall Scheme provides a mechanism for
such claims to be investigated.

of historical practices, in both a civil and criminal context.

Questions addressed

What was the scope of KPMG‘s review, its limitations and findings?

Report

1. KPMG were instructed to perform a review into Post Office’s historical operation of suspense
accounts. The scope of this review was as follows:

e Conduct research into historical suspense account operating practices pre-March 2019,
holding discussions with key individuals and reviewing relevant documentation (where
still available) to:

i. Identify any additional relevant suspense accounts to the 4 which were identified
during their review of Post Office’s current operation of suspense accounts.

ii. Identify any changes in the suspense account operating processes during the
relevant time period, which would significantly alter the way the identified accounts
operated and whether these changes could have potentially had an adverse impact
on the Postmasters; and

iii. Understand whether the historical resolution processes adopted by Post office for
dealing with amounts posted into these suspense accounts were sufficient to
identify potential instances where amounts should have been reconciled against
branch discrepancies made good by Postmaster.

e Undertake historical analysis of balances held within the relevant suspense accounts for
the relevant time period (where data was still available) to establish whether postings
to these accounts have altered, and if so the potential impact.

e Perform a high level review of the Tier 2 investigation data arising over the past 12
months to inform their understanding of how amounts could get posted to the relevant
suspense accounts

KPMG’s Findings

2. KPMG’‘s review into how Post Office currently operates the relevant’ suspense accounts
concluded that, given the robust and transparent investigations process that is undertaken,
these suspense accounts should not result in Post Office pursuing Postmasters for sums it
had or could eventually take to profit (nor had any evidence been presented to indicate
Post Office had). This was because sums housed in these suspense accounts were either:

e not taken to a profit and loss account; or
e relate to unmatched transactions due to customers (not postmasters); or
¢ relate to surpluses rather than shortfalls.

3. KPMG’s review into the historical practices did not identify any additional relevant suspense
accounts to those which formed part of their review of current practices. Further, KPMG
were informed by Post Office employees, including the product team leaders responsible
for posting amounts to the relevant suspense accounts that no changes had been made in
the operating processes since they had been established?.

4. As such, KPMG‘s overarching finding from its review into how Post Office currently operates
these suspense accounts should therefore also apply to the historical operation of these
suspense accounts, though it should be recognised, as you would expect, Post Office’s
investigation into discrepancies has evolved and improved, with the introduction of the Tier
Two Investigation Team.

5. The two relevant suspense accounts identified by KPMG are the Agent Creditor Suspense
Account (ACSA) and the Customer Creditor Suspense Account (CCSA). Taking these in turn:

e The ACSA holds surplus discrepancies which Postmasters (as opposed to Post Office)
dispute being due back to them. In respect of this account, KPMG’s finding from its
review of historical practices is that is has seen no evidence to indicate amounts posted
to this account related to shortfalls which should have been repaid to Postmasters.

« The CCSA should only have items posted to it once it has been determined that they
relate to outstanding customer funds (as opposed to Postmaster shortfalls). Thus, branch
affecting discrepancies should not be included within this account, which is a holding
account for customer’s money. KPMG's finding is that overall, a robust resolution process
appears to have been in place for each product type that is posted into this account. This
process identified instances where amounts needed to be reconciled against branch
discrepancies prior to them being posted into this account However, KPMG’s has
identified two exceptions:

a. Where Postmasters have accepted cheques made out to Post Office as payment
for certain services but have incorrectly recorded this transaction as having been

+ Accounts into which Post Office places sums that could relate to discrepancies at a branch level and from which unmatched
sums are taken into the P&L account

2 It should be noted however that there is no formal documentation detailing what operational processes were or were not in

paid for in cash and the supporting information (which would include branch

b. When, between November 2015 and April 2019, Postmasters failed to follow the
then prescribed two part cancellation process for MoneyGram Transactions, which
also coincided with unrelated connectivity issues (‘MoneyGram Issue”)

Each of these are discussed in further detail below.

6. The CCSA was established in April 2010 and the ACSA was established in January 2012.
Prior to the establishment of the ACSA, such surpluses remained on Postmasters personal
accounts, which were not released to Post Office’s P&L account and remained on that
account until claimed by a Postmaster. Prior to establishment of the CCSA, the Client
Creditor Suspense Account was used to house unmatched customer monies (as well as
unmatched client monies). KPMG performed a high level review of transactions posted to
Client Creditor Suspense Account from 2005 (the earliest date for which data is available)
to 2020 and held discussions with the relevant Post Office employees who manage this
account. Limited documentation is available for the Client Creditor Suspense Account prior
to 2018, but no further potential issues to those noted above were identified by KPMG.

The MoneyGram Issue: Postmasters failing to follow the two part cancellation
process requirements for MoneyGram Transactions and unrelated connectivity
problems

7. In November 2015, Post Office made changes to the how MoneyGram transactions were
cancelled on Horizon. Postmasters were required to perform a two part cancellation process
whereby the transaction had to be reversed through the Postmasters till (the Horizon EPOS)
and also cancelled within the MoneyGram interface (also within the Horizon EPOS).

8. In April 2019 Post Office changed the process so that both elements were linked into a one
part cancellation process.

9. KPMG were informed by the Post Office MoneyGram product team that during the same
time period, MoneyGram transactions experienced connectivity issues.

10.The changes to the MoneyGram cancellation process resulted in an increased number of
errors being made by Postmasters when cancelling MoneyGram transactions. In addition,
connectivity issues resulted in an increase in unmatched MoneyGram balances that the
MoneyGram product team needed to investigate.

11.Post Office subsequently stood up a team of 8 individuals who undertook a large-scale
investigation with MoneyGram to try to resolve these unmatched items and identify

customers and Postmasters who may be due money back as a result of these issues. This
investigation ran during 2017 and 2018.

Confidential

Post Office Limited - Risk and Compliance Committee-12/11/20 103 of 226

Tab 5 Suspense Accounts

@

12.All MoneyGram transactions are date stamped, with each posting also including the branch
FAD code. As such, for each MoneyGram transaction, it was possible to identify the branch
where it was performed and when.

13.For each unmatched MoneyGram transaction, the investigation team reviewed whether
there was any contemporaneous data from the branches identified to indicate that a
MoneyGram transaction had not been cancelled in accordance with the two part process.

14.Where the data showed a MoneyGram transaction had been cancelled on Horizon but had
not also been cancelled on the MoneyGram interface, on the date in question, transaction
corrections were issued to move the loss to the Postmaster. The transaction correction
included enough detail for the Postmaster to correct the original error if the money had not
been collected by the recipient.

15.Where the data showed a MoneyGram transaction had not been reversed on Horizon but
had been cancelled on the MoneyGram interface on the date in question and where the
investigations team could identify the issue related to a branch error a transaction
corrections was issued to repay Postmasters.

16.Where the data did not indicate a branch error or indicated a customer loss the unmatched
sums were posted to the Customer Creditor Account.

17.Investigations did not include speaking to branches unless Postmasters contacted Post
office, regarding MoneyGram related discrepancies.

18. Enquiries have been made with individuals still at Post Office who were part of the 8 person
investigation team, including the Team Leader. Although weekly MI was said to be
produced, reporting on progress, none has been able to be produced owing to emails not
being retained (though they would likely be available via the mimecast archive). It has not
been possible therefore to determine to total amount returned to Postmasters.

19.What has been determined is that £615,178 could not be matched and was posted to the
CCSA in 2017. This was split across 1,804 branches with 97% having a balance of less than
£2,000 and the largest balance being £5,400.

20.Given the level of investigation undertaken by a separate team, the ease with which an
affected branch and potential branch shortfall could be identified and the level of exposure
at each branch, KPMG‘s view is that the risk that the £615,178 includes a significant balance

relating to Postmasters is low and this balance is more likely to relate to customer losses.
However, the lack of available documentation means a residual risk remains.

Bulk Post Office Cheques Issue: Postmasters Incorrectly Processing Cheques as
Cash

21.A number of products Post Office offers can be paid for through cheques which are made
payable to Post Office (as opposed to the client) For instance DVLA payments, These are
known as “Bulk Post Office Cheques”. There is a defined process for accepting such cheques

5

104 of 226 Post Office Limited - Risk and Compliance Committee-12/11/20

POL-BSFF-0238386_0103
POL00423571

Tab 5 Suspense Accounts

@

and then sending them to Post Office to process which, if followed correctly, should identify
any Postmaster Bulk Post Office cheque processing errors, with a Transaction Correction
subsequently issued.

22.However, if a Postmaster accepts a Bulk Post Office Cheque as payment but, in error,
records that the transaction was paid for in cash, more cash will be expected in the till than
is physically there.

23.1f when then sending the Bulk Post Office Cheque to Post Office, the branch information
which should accompany the cheque also becomes separated from the cheque - the
payment is made to the recipient but as there is no matching transaction on Horizon (i.e.
as it was processed as cash) it is difficult for the Cheques Team to identify which branch
has made the error and for a Transaction Correction to be issued.

24.If Postmasters realise the error they have made, they are able to contact the Cheques Team
to rectify the error. Further, the Cheques Team proactively attempt to identify branches
where there are high value (>£500) Bulk Post Office cheques with no supporting branch
information, but if the branch is not identified, the cheque value is subsequently posted to
the CCSA.

25.c£134k has been posted to the CCSA as a result of Bulk Post Office Cheques Issues since
it was established in 2010. Although a proportion of this sum could relate to branch
shortfalls caused by Postmaster error, it will also include:

i. Customer losses: e.g. a Bulk Post Office Cheque is processed but a corresponding
bill payment is not made / not made in full (for example, a DVLA transaction is not
processed through the till on Horizon or the DVLA interface but the cheque is
accepted and sent for processing. The cheque is banked by POL but DVLA will not
process the transaction or request payment as it is not on their system

ii. Matching Errors: e.g. the transaction is recorded correctly but the Bulk Post Office
Cheque becomes separated from its supporting information when being sent to Post
Office for processing. The customer's payment is correctly made, the Postmaster’s
till will balance but when the cheque payment comes to be allocated to a client,
there is no way of knowing which client should be credited as the cheque is made
out to Post Office.

26.KPMG's understanding is that it is not possible to identify what proportion of the £134k is
made up from matching errors, customer losses or potential Postmaster losses due to the
lack of available supporting information documented on the cheque.

POST OFFICE LIMITED
RISK & COMPLIANCE COMMITTEE REPORT

Title: Duplicate Transaction Corrections I weeting pate: I 12 November 2020
7 Tim Perkins, Head of Security, . Al Cameron, Group Chief Finance
Ractive Safety & Loss Prevention Spuruats Officer

Input Sought: Noting

The Committee is asked to note an incident on 14/10/2020 where 571 cash surplus / shortage
duplicate Transaction Corrections were issued to 449 Branches in error along with the steps
taken to remediate the issue and avoid a reoccurrence.

Executive Summary

On 15/10/2020 it became known that 571 cash surplus / shortage Transaction Corrections
(TCs) had been duplicated. This was brought to light by several calls from branches to the
Branch Reconciliation Team (BRT) and the National Cash Centre Accounts & Reconciliation
Team (NCCART), which led to an investigation. The cause was quickly identified as an individual
within BRT who had bypassed the quality control mechanism which is for each batch of TCs to
be reviewed and signed off by a manager. In this case, the first issue of the TCs on 12/10/2020
had bypassed the quality control check, but the second time these were issued, the individual
followed the correct process.

To remediate this, all 449 branches were immediately sent a memoview (Appendix A) to
acknowledge and apologise for the error. A further TC to correct the error was arranged and
sent the same day 15/10/2020 to correct the branch accounts.

The incident was reported to the Historical Matters Unit for information.

A Branch Focus article was issued to the entire network along with the incident being included
in Amanda Jones’ regular communication to branches to remind branches to report anything
that looks strange or that they do not understand and to assure them we will listen and
investigate. (Appendix B)

The matter is being dealt with under the Conduct Code with the individual concerned, with the
first step being a full fact-find.

In parallel, investigations are underway with Accenture to establish whether a failsafe can be
introduced to prevent either the QA bypass or duplicate TCs being issued.

Confidential

Post Office Limited - Risk and Compliance Committee-12/11/20 107 of 226

POL-BSFF-0238386_0106
POL00423571
POL00423571

Tab 6 Notification of Transaction Correction error

@

Questions addressed

1. How did this incident occur?
2. What steps were put in place to remediate the incident?

3. What further steps are being taken to prevent such an incident happening again?

Report

4. An incident occurred where 571 duplicate TCs were issued to 449 branches as a result of
an individual failing to follow the quality control process. In bypassing this process, the
individual sent the first batch without the prescribed check by a manager within the Branch
Reconciliation Team and also failed to check these off as a completed task.

5. The second time the TCs were issued, all relevant checks were made, but these checks
are designed to ensure accuracy of the TCs and the associated wording for branches and
would not check whether the TCs had already been issued since this should not be possible
under the process if followed correctly.

6. The incident came to light due to calls from branches which allowed a speedy investigation
and a remediation plan to be put in place.

7. The branches concerned were sent a memoview to explain what had happened and to
apologise for the incident. Appendix A

8. Additionally, the Historical Matters Unit were informed due to potential repercussions
within the context of GLO.

9. A branch-wide communication was issued on 15/10/2020 to report the incident to all
branches to ensure transparency of this issue since there is no fail-safe in place today.
This communication also reminded branches to contact the telephone number on TCs if
they have any concerns as a reassurance to them, although we know this issue was
contained to the 449 branches only.

10. The issue is being dealt with under the Conduct Code for the individual concerned and it
is expected the full fact find will identify improvement opportunities which will be
implemented over the next weeks and months, along with further supervisory checks.

11. Additionally, Accenture have been engaged to explore a technical fail-safe to either
prevent management controls being ignored or over-ridden, or preventing duplicate TCs
being issued.

12. These last two points are to be pursued by Alison Bolsover and Colette McAteer who
manage the Branch Reconciliation Team and have been added to their Q3 objectives.

Risk Assessment, Mitigations & Legal Implications

13. Post Office is addressing this issue consistently with the duties implied by the GLO
Common Issues Judgment into postmaster contracts, through its communications to the
affected branches and wider network, and by ensuring that affected postmasters are not
held liable for any financial impact the issue may have had to their branch accounts.

14. Changes were made to Post Office processes and controls relating to the management of
TCs as part of Network Operation’s remediation response to the Common Issues
Judgment. The incident outlined in this paper demonstrates the importance of ensuring
that those processes and controls are subject to ongoing review, monitoring and
assurance.

Confidential

108 of 226 Post Office Limited - Risk and Compliance Committee-12/11/20

POL00423571

Tab 6 Notification of Transaction Correction error

Stakeholder Implications

15. The impacted stakeholders in this issue were Postmasters. The actions taken to
communicate and resolve the issue mitigated the impact on Postmasters and, to date, no
complaints have been raised about the remediation actions taken by Post Office.

Next Steps & Timelines

16. The two actions in place; 1) the full fact find into the incident as part of the Conduct Code
and 2) the investigation into a technical solution via Accenture, to be completed by 30
November. In the event of a technical solution being preferred, this will be established as

a Prove plan / short project for consideration by PRB and sponsored by Amanda Jones /
Dan Zinner.

MBS 273 Duplicate Transaction Corrections

For information

We have become aware today that Transaction Corrections issued to your branch for Cash Pouches on 12 October
have been duplicated on 14 October. We are sorry for this duplication.

We will be issuing compensating Transaction Corrections today which will appear on Horizon tomorrow for you to
accept.

We can only apologise for any inconvenience this has caused to you.

Tab 6 Notification of Transaction Correction error

Appendix 2

Extract from Amanda Jones’ email to branches sent 22 October 2020

Duplicate Transaction Corrections
Last week some branches received duplicate Transaction Corrections for Cash surpluses/shortages in error. Thank
you to branches who called us to raise this issue. The team identified the problem and a compensating Transaction
Correction was issued on Horizon for acceptance on Friday 16 October to correct the situation.

The duplicate Transaction Corrections was a result of human error within the Branch Reconciliation team who issue
Transaction Corrections. The controls that prevent this type of error occurring are urgently being reviewed and we'll
be making changes to improve the process. Mistakes like this do happen, and we’ll be open about them with you.

If you receive a Transaction Correction notice and have a question or are concerned about it, the notice will include a
phone number to contact for more information. We've provided a full update in this week’s branch focus.

Extract from Branch Focus - 22 October 2020

Transaction Corrections - Guidance

This is to notify Postmasters that last week 449 branches were issued duplicate Transaction Corrections for
Cash surpluses/shortages in error. This was as a result of human error within the Branch Reconciliation team
who issue Transaction Corrections.

As soon as we were aware of this error, we sent a memoview (MBS 273) to the affected branches. This explains
that the Transaction Corrections issued on Monday 12 October had been duplicated in error on Wednesday 14
October (appearing on Horizon on Thursday 15 October). A compensating Transaction Correction was issued to
appear on Horizon for acceptance on Friday 16 October to correct the situation.

The controls that should prevent this type of error occurring are under urgent review and will be updated and
amended where necessary, including retraining. However, if you are ever uncomfortable with a Transaction
Correction or the reason for it isn’t clear, please do call the telephone number which is always provided on the
Transaction Correction narrative to report this so we can investigate and make sure it is correct.

Thank you to those branches who did call us about this error. This enabled us to quickly identify the duplication
issue and resolve it for the branches concerned.

Thank you for your support and we apologise for the inconvenienced caused by this issue.

Confidential

Post Office Limited - Risk and Compliance Committee-12/11/20 111 of 226

POST OFFICE LIMITED
RISK & COMPLIANCE COMMITTEE REPORT

Title: Financial Reporting Controls Meeting Date: I 12 November 2020
Environment
Christine Kirby, Financial Controls taal a
Author: Manager & Tom Lee, Financial Sponsor: Alledatr Ganapren, Group Chine
Finance Officer
Controller

Input Sought: Noting

The Risk & Compliance Committee (“RCC”) is asked to note the update on the Financial
Reporting Controls (“FRC”) environment, including improvements made since the last report to
the Audit, Risk and Compliance Committee (“ARC”) in 2019 and details on future focus, for
onward submission to the ARC.

Previous Governance Oversight
e Prior year report was presented to ARC for noting on 23 September 2019.

Executive Summary

Since the last report to the RCC and ARC, the FRCF has continued to be improved through
process reviews and controls testing. This has enabled the FRCF to become more streamlined
and risk focused. Looking forward, the aim is for the FRCF to transfer to ServiceNow (or another
provider) in order to be more agile, efficient and to create synergies between Central Risk and
Financial Controls, as well as allowing PricewaterhouseCoopers LLP (“PwC”) to mitigate their
threat of self-review, as Post Office Limited (“POL”) currently use a PwC off the shelf tool called
TrAction which has been in place for 3 years.

The FRC environment has continued to be strengthened through enhancements in the biannual
Balance Sheet testing, quarterly Finance Director (“FD”) Balance Sheet reviews and Balance
Sheet reconciliation review processes. A finance working group has been established, in order
to drive improvements and efficiencies, specifically in relation to balance sheet related
processes, controls and responsibilities.

Confidential

112 of 226 Post Office Limited - Risk and Compliance Committee-12/11/20

POL-BSFF-0238386_0111
POL00423571
POL00423571

Tab 7 Financial Reporting Controls Framework Update

Questions addressed

1. What was the status of the FRC environment when last reported to ARC?

2. What is the current status of the Financial Reporting Controls Framework (“FRCF”)?
3. What developments have been made to the wider FRC environment?

4. What do independent sources say about the FRC environment?

5. What is the future focus?

Report
Past and current status of the FRCF

6. The last report to ARC was in September 2019 at which point there were 500 controls split
across 26 processes, which addressed 535 individual risks. See Appendix 2 for more
details.

7. Throughout the year the Financial Controls team have performed end to end process
reviews across 8 of the highest risk and/or oldest processes, scoping out those which were
created/reviewed relatively recently as part of wider projects.

8. This has resulted in streamlining the controls framework. The FRCF now holds 438
controls, split across 26 processes, which address 306 risks.

9. The process reviews enabled risks to be streamlined through removal of bespoke risks
and focus on Financial Statement Line Item level risks which are more widely applicable.
This ensures that the FRCF is aligned clearly with its purpose of ensuring the Financial
Statements are not misstated due to error or fraud.

10. The remaining processes are being reviewed on a rolling basis, prioritising the highest risk
processes. Risk assessment is based on materiality, risk of fraud and level of manual
intervention, along with any known issues which come to light.

11. Of the 438 controls, 346 controls were issued for self-assessment in September 2020. The
remaining controls were either not operated due to frequency (i.e. annual, biannual,
quarterly) or were control gaps (a risk has been identified and control has been
designed but is not yet in operation). See Appendix 1 for more details.

12. Of the 346 controls issues for self-assessment; 96% were operating effectively (2019:
91%). The remaining 4% includes 1% of controls not self-assessed due to illness and 3%
of controls self-assessed but not operating. Reasons for controls not operating include

resource issues, delay in meeting deadlines and requests to change control ownership. All
controls not operating are followed up and investigated.

Confidential 2

Post Office Limited - Risk and Compliance Committee-12/11/20 113 of 226

POL-BSFF-0238386_0112
POL00423571
POL00423571

Tab 7 Financial Reporting Controls Framework Update

@

13. The results of the Control Self-Assessment (“CSA”) are reported to the Chief Financial
Officer (“CFO”) on a monthly basis, whilst providing an update on the wider FRC
environment.

14. In the prior year there were 28 control gaps, as at September 2020 there are 10 control
gaps. These are all classed as medium or low risk due to having mitigating controls and/or
due to being non-key controls. The remaining gaps are largely due to system limitations
and are therefore expected to be long term gaps.

15. Controls testing is performed by the Financial Controls team on an annual basis to ensure
each control is tested back to auditable evidence at least once a year. If a control does
not meet the expected standard, it is retested again within the year. The controls testing
results have improved year on year thanks to the feedback control owners gain through
the testing process.

Wider FRC environment

16. Balance Sheet reconciliations for standard risks GLs are submitted to Central Finance on
a quarterly basis and high risks GL reconciliations are submitted on a monthly basis. The
reduction of standard risk GL reconciliations to quarterly frequency is in part thanks to
improved reconciliation quality which has been a focus over the past 18 months.

17. Detailed Balance Sheet testing is performed by Central Finance on a biannual basis on P6
and P11/P12 balances. The GLs in scope for testing are selected based on risk rating. This
testing involves agreeing the GL balances back to auditable evidence in order to assess if
any adjustments are required.

18. FD Balance Sheet review files are prepared by Central Finance and shared with the
respective business units for their review on a monthly basis. FD Balance Sheet review
meetings are held on a quarterly basis to enable Central Finance and Commercial Finance
teams to place greater scrutiny over the Balance Sheets for each area.

19. A finance working group has been established to drive improvements and efficiencies,
specifically in relation to balance sheet related processes, controls and responsibilities. A
focus list has been established and work is under way to enact change in a swift and
positive manner with stakeholders from across finance fully engaged. Changes include
enhancements to underlying chart of accounts, establishing clear roles and responsibilities
and improving reporting processes.

20. During the year, the second annual Grant Thornton LLP (“GT”) audit over third parties
whose data POL rely on for revenue recognition took place. The scope of the audit was
data analysis, IT general controls testing and business process controls testing across
POL's largest service providers (e.g. Fujitsu Telecoms, BGL, DXC, JP Morgan). No
significant auditing findings or recommendations were identified. All minor observations
were fed back to the service providers and have been subsequently actioned.

Confidential 3

114 of 226 Post Office Limited - Risk and Compliance Committee-12/11/20

POL-BSFF-0238386_0113
POL00423571
POL00423571

Tab 7 Financial Reporting Controls Framework Update

@

21. Since the last internal audit of the FRCF (May 2019), there have been several internal
audits which involved substantive testing of controls in the FRCF including; post BOT
controls, accounts receivable controls, minimum controls standard (COVID-19 impact),
manual postings to revenue. These audits have identified control recommendations for
the relevant business units but no items have highlighted material issues within the FRCF.

Assurance over the FRCF

22. The most recent internal audit, manual postings to revenue, tested controls which had
already been tested by the Financial Controls team and found no difference in the results
of the testing. This provides assurance over the quality of the Financial Controls team
testing.

24. The external audit identified minimal management letter points, none of which concerned
the FRCF or other key FRC related processes discussed in this report. The audit findings
were principally related to differences of opinion on judgemental areas or the classification
of balance sheet items, neither of which are indicators of issues within the FRC
environment.

Governance Risk and Compliance tool

25. The FRCF is currently held on a tool called TrAction, administrated by PwC. Due to
increased independence rules, PwC are unable to perform non-audit services for audit
clients and therefore plan to cease POL’s use of TrAction by April 2022 latest.

26. POL have been looking to move to a new Governance Risk and Compliance (“GRC”) tool
for some time due to the system limitations of TrAction but this had been placed on hold
due to budget constraints.

27. The timing of POL’s requirement to cease use of TrAction has fallen in line with the Central
Risk team’s plans to move to ServiceNow for top down risk assessment from January
2021. In order to work towards a POL wide governance framework being on one GRC tool,
the FRCF will migrate to ServiceNow as phase 2 of the same project, with the aim of being
live in the system by end of March 2021, budget dependent.

28. This move will reduce manual actions (i.e. through approval workflows and automated
notifications), enable audit trail for changes and improve management reporting through
availability of comparative data. The shared tool will also help create synergies between
the FRCF and the top down risk assessment as the self-assessments for the FRCF will roll
up into the risks maintained by the Central Risk team.

Next Steps & Timelines

29. As well as acquiring a new GRC tool, the Financial Controls team are performing a FRCF
completeness review to ensure all financial reporting risks are mitigated. The review is a
lengthy process and is due to be finalised in FY20/21. The team also continue to perform
end to end process reviews to ensure that the FRCF remains up to date. The next
processes under review are loss prevention, record to report and bank & cash
management.

30. Continual development of the BS Recs and associated FD BS Review process in order to
get the right balance between independent central review and ownership by divisional
finance teams. In order to assist with this, we are investigating the potential of acquiring
a balance sheet reporting tool which would aide workflow, reporting and risk
identification.

31. The third party revenue data audit performed by GT is ideally going to be brought in house
for FY20/21. The internal resource will be an Internal Audit Senior Manager (3A) and a
Financial Controls specialist (3B), both roles of which are currently out in the market. The

roles will focus on performing data analysis, IT controls testing and business process
controls testing.

Controle Control Gaps [control Owners Tepk 2000 CoA Resuls (avbmied ODOT)
Contrate I95365™2%l controls withI Not operated I Nose’ I Controle not

Explanation of key columns:

« Control gaps - a risk has been identified and control has been designed but is not
yet in operation. Mitigating controls are in place.

e Controls operated effectively - controls which have been self-assessed in the
period as operating as per the control wording.

e Self-assessment submitted but control not operated - controls which did not
operate effectively in the period. The system prompts the control owner to create
a remediation plan if they select that their control is not operating and these are
followed up by CF.

« Controls with long term remediations - controls which are not operating effectively
and there is no short-term solution. These controls are classes as ‘gaps’ and
mitigating controls are put in place.

« No self-assessment submitted - controls where the owner has not completed the
control self-assessment in the given period. These are followed up by CF.

e Controls not included due to frequency - controls which are not operated on a
monthly basis (i.e. annual, bi-annual and quarterly controls) and were not
expected to be operated in the period being reported on.

RISK & COMPLIANCE COMMITTEE REPORT

PwC Agreed Upon Procedures

Title: Meeting Date: I 12 November 2020

Brian Kelly, Finance Director - Tax,
Author: Treasury and Working Capital & Sponsor:
Tom Lee, Financial Controller

Alisdair Cameron, Group Chief
Finance Officer

Input Sought: Noting

The Committee is asked to note:
i. the scope, purpose and status of the Agreed Upon Procedure (“AUP”) engagements
performed by PricewaterhouseCoopers LLP (“PwC”)
ii. the findings and recommendations made by PwC
iii. the actions performed by Post Office Limited (“POL”) in response to PwC's finding
for onward submission to the Audit, Risk & Compliance Committee (ARC).

Executive Summary

Several contractual arrangements which POL have with third parties require POL’s external
auditors to perform AUP engagements to review specific processes, controls and policies in
place at POL with regards to each stakeholders interest. These partners are as follows:

Department for Business, Energy and Industrial Strategy (“BEIS”)
Bank of England (“BoE”)

Royal Mail (“RM”)

Driver and Vehicle Licensing Agency (“DVLA”)

The BEIS and RM reports are being finalised and should be complete by the end of November
2020. No material issues are expected.

The DVLA and BoE reports have been shared with each of the stakeholders. The stakeholders
have confirmed that they are comfortable with the findings noted.

The Treasury team have put in place an action plan to deal with the BoE recommendations,
most of which have already been completed. The remaining actions will be closed off by the
end of November 2020.

Confidential

Post Office Limited - Risk and Compliance Committee-12/11/20 119 of 226

Tab 8 Agreed Upon Procedures

Questions addressed

1. What is the scope and status of the AUP engagements performed by PwC?
2. What were the findings and recommendations raised by PwC?

3. What actions have been performed by POL in response to the PwC findings?

Report
Overview of AUP engagements performed by PwC

4. BEIS AUP - review the accuracy of the financial covenant calculations which are reported
throughout the year to BEIS and compare the reported position, for a sample of months
across the financial year, to the management information from which they were obtained.

5. BoE AUP - provide a report on Post Office Limited’s processes surrounding the Notes
Circulation Scheme (NCS) and on the suitability of design and the operating effectiveness
of its controls for the financial year under review.

6. RM AUP - comparison of revenue figures reported weekly to RM back to underlying
financial records and cash receipts, for the financial year under review.

7. DVLA AUP - comparison of the annual statement provided to DVLA, showing the volume
of DVLA transactions performed in branches, back to underlying financial records.

BEIS AUP

8. PwC performed agreed the financial covenant calculations, produced and reported on a
monthly basis, back to the data sources from which the information was obtained. The
calculations were reperformed and understanding of compliance procedures surrounding
the calculation was obtained. The procedures were designed to ensure that POL has met
the requirements around calculations and reporting under the BEIS loan agreement for
the financial year under review.

9. The PwC report is yet to be finalised but based on discussions between POL and PwC no
significant issues have been identified. The only item to note, which will be included in the
PwC report, is that the reports provided to BEIS are based on numbers at a specific point
in time and are not retrospectively amended. This has no impact on period 1 to 11 as the
report is produced at month end close, with any late adjustments being minor. However,
at yearend the final numbers may not be agreed until months after the report is issued
which could lead to inconsistencies e.g. for FY19/20 the Annual Report and Accounts
(“ARA”) is yet to be finalised and therefore the numbers reported to BEIS in April 2020
may vary considerably to what will ultimately appear in the ARA. A reconciliation will be
included in the PwC report to reconcile the reported position to the latest draft ARA.

Tab 8 Agreed Upon Procedures

@

10. This is not deemed to be an issue as the adjustments made would not, in the instances
identified, cause a breach in the covenants. If a breach was to occur then BEIS would be
notified as part of the routine ongoing management interactions.

11. The BEIS AUP report is expected to be signed in November 2020.

BoE AUP

12. The report is produced to provide an understanding and examination of the policies and
procedures designed and implemented by Post Office to provide user entities with Note
Circulation Scheme services (NCS). It looks at the Suitability of Design and Operating
Effectiveness of its Controls for the period from 1 April 2019 to 31 March 2020.

13. Continued membership of the NCS Scheme is dependent on meeting the Bank’s reporting
requirements as well as its security standards.

14. The Bank of England Rules on the Note Circulation Scheme defines how Note Recirculation
Facility (NRF) declarations should be calculated.

15. PwC reported 28 individual findings in their audit of the NCS scheme (see Appendix 1), 12
of which were because of evidence not being available due to COVID-19 restricting access
to sites and therefore preventing physical inspection.

16. BoE have reviewed the reported findings and confirmed with POL at the October 2020
Quarterly Compliance call that they have no concerns about the findings due to their
immateriality.

17. No material control failures were highlighted, however, management recognise the need
to take action to ensure Post Office is fully compliant with the rules of the scheme and
maintain a good relationship with the Bank of England. The findings have been
summarised at a high level in paragraphs 18 and 19. The detailed list of individual findings
can be found in appendix 1.

18. Staff Knowledge, Training and Access:

e Limited evidence of staff training in the team;

e A-user access list for Team Drive, SharePoint and the NIPS system could not be
obtained for the full year;

e No evidence was provided to demonstrate that a review of the NIPS system user
access list was performed in the year;

e The spreadsheets used to calculate the daily NFR position should all be password
protected.

19. Other findings:
e Signed audit packs and CCTV footage from cash centres which validate key controls
have been performed could not be accessed due to restrictions in place for COVID-
19.
e Multiple instances when the Cash Centre Managers did not confirm the daily forecast
as per the agreed control

Tab 8 Agreed Upon Procedures

@

20. Whilst the findings are not material, management recognise the need to address the
findings and have agreed the actions in Appendix 2 with the Treasury team. Most of the
actions have been completed, with the remaining due to be completed in November 2020.
Note the findings will appear in the FY20/21 PwC report, despite being cleared, as they
will have been applicable for part of the financial year under review.

RM AUP

21. PwC performed a review of POL’s gross actual revenue for the year ending 29 March 2020.
This involved casting the ‘breakdown schedule’, agreeing the schedules to the POL General
Ledger and agreed the total gross revenue to the bank statement.

22. One exception was identified where the gross revenue per the GL and per the bank
statement was greater than the ‘breakdown schedule’ by c. £10m. This was an
overpayment to Royal Mail which was subsequently returned the following week -
management were aware, rectified this at the time and no further instances have occurred
as a result of controls in place.

23. As part of POL's requirements to provide all relevant information to PwC which could
impact RM revenue, POL also notified PwC of an issue regarding the application of new
stamp tariffs which occurred in Feb 2019, resulting in under settlement of c. £500k to RM
which was resolved post year end. This was noted in PwC's report but RM were fully aware
and subsequent settlement has been performed.

24. POL has also informed PwC about the stamps review (Project Frank) performed in the year
by KPMG LLP. PwC are due to sign a hold harmless letter to enable them to review the
KPMG report directly to ensure there is no impact on the AUP report or managements
associated representation letter. Once this has been closed off the PwC AUP report will be
finalised for signing, expected to be November 2020.

DVLA AUP

25. PwC performed walkthroughs of the end to end DVLA transaction process, reconciliation
of SAP data to Horizon data, sampling transactions to ensure they flowed through from
Horizon to the bank statement, analytical review of sales and remittances and
reperformance of key calculations driving the Annual (volumes) Statement provided to
DVLA.

26. PwC identified some minor findings in relation to daily reconciliation variances, which were
subsequently resolved, and sizable movements in sales balances in branches, which were
suitably explained. Additionally, an IT finding was included as a general point around user
access controls, which was noted in the main yearend ARA audit and included in this report
for completeness as it was in relation to branch system access controls.

27. Any reconciling differences noted were trivial (less than £5k) and ‘unusual movements’
noted in the analytical reviews have been backed up by management responses.

28. The report has been shared with DVLA and no issues have been raised by management.

Conclusion

29. Whilst PwC identified findings through their AUPs, POL management are comfortable that
these are immaterial and the overall outcomes of the AUPs demonstrate a strong control
environment across the business processes.

30. Any remaining findings and recommendations will be addressed where required ahead of
the FY20/21 AUP reviews.

Next Steps & Timelines

31. BOE - The remaining actions in relation to the BoE NCS AUP will be closed by 27 November
2020.

32. RM - PwC are due to sign the KPMG hold harmless letter so that they can review the Stamps
report and ensure that there is no impact prior to releasing the AUP report. Report expected
to be signed in November 2020.

33. BEIS - final review and clearances are underway and the report is expected to be signed
in November 2020.

34. DVLA - None.

Graham Hemingway, Historical

Author: Matters Portfolio Lead and Chris Sponsor: Declan Salter, GLO Director
Manamley, HM Finance Director

Input Sought: Noting & Discussion

The Committee is asked to note and discuss the approached to Governance on Historical
Matters (HM) for onward submission to the Audit, Risk & Compliance Committee (RCC).

Executive Summary

HM was set up during September as a ring-fenced Business Unit in order to centralise all
the work emerging from the three judgments under a new Director reporting to the Group
CEO and Chair; to allow Post Office Executive to concentrate upon current and future
business; and to ensure all current procedures are reflective of a new culture that will
prevent such Historical events every occurring again.

A revised governance structure has been based upon core elements of the existing Strategic
Portfolio Office (SPO) framework with changes to the approval processes. Key elements, as
discussed with the CFO and team are:

Budgeting and forecasting costs

- These are aligned to the current Post Office Limited (POL) budgeting and forecasting
procedures

Financial reporting and accruals

- No Changes made to process used previously
- Reporting improvements made by Finance team

Procurement

- POL procurement processes are used wherever appropriate and Procurement team
have supported on legal cost negotiations

Financial communications with UKGI/BEIS-

- HM is to feed information via POL Finance to ensure data consistency and single point
of contact

Controls, both financial and over data accuracy

- No changes have been made to control procedures.

Internal audit
